1. Containerization Layer

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer turns the existing Django backend and React frontend application code into hardened, portable, reproducible OCI images that can be built in CI, scanned, signed, pushed to Amazon ECR, and deployed to Kubernetes using Helm.

It includes:

Backend image for Django

Frontend image for React static assets served via NGINX

Multi-stage Docker builds

Non-root runtime

Health checks

Versioned immutable tags

SBOM and vulnerability scanning inputs

Supply-chain hardening controls


At enterprise scale, containerization is not just packaging. It is the first security boundary, the first reproducibility boundary, and the foundation for CI/CD, GitOps, rollback, compliance, and runtime reliability.


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

Before infrastructure, Kubernetes, CI, GitOps, and observability can work safely, the application images must be:

deterministic

minimal

secure

scan-friendly

immutable

portable across environments and regions


If this layer is weak, every higher layer inherits the weakness.

Examples:

A bloated image increases attack surface and pull time.

A root-based container increases lateral movement risk.

A mutable latest tag breaks rollback integrity.

Missing health checks cause bad pods to stay in service.

Unpinned dependencies create non-reproducible releases.


Containerization is therefore the supply-chain entry point and the artifact contract between engineering, platform, security, and operations.


---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

In a FAANG-level or highly regulated enterprise, containerization decisions are made with these goals:

1. Security first

Images must run as non-root, use minimal base layers, avoid package manager residue, and expose as little OS surface as possible. Every extra shell utility is another risk surface.

2. Reproducibility

A given git commit must produce a predictable artifact. That artifact must be traceable by:

git SHA

CI build number

SBOM

scan report

ECR digest


3. Fast deployments and fast rollback

Smaller images pull faster, start faster, and reduce blast radius during rolling updates. Rollback must reference a prior immutable image digest, not a re-used tag.

4. Auditability

Auditors care about:

what was deployed

when it was built

what dependencies it contained

whether vulnerabilities were assessed

whether secrets were baked into the image


5. Compatibility with GitOps

GitOps expects image references to be declarative and immutable. The container strategy must support promotion by tag or digest, not mutable human convention.

6. Multi-region readiness

If prod runs in two regions, images should be available in region-local ECR repositories or via ECR replication so failover does not depend on a distant registry pull path.


---

D. Folder structure

A realistic repository layout for the application layer:

repo-root/
├── backend/
│   ├── app/
│   │   ├── manage.py
│   │   ├── project/
│   │   ├── requirements/
│   │   │   ├── base.txt
│   │   │   ├── prod.txt
│   │   │   └── dev.txt
│   │   └── ...
│   ├── Dockerfile
│   ├── .dockerignore
│   ├── gunicorn.conf.py
│   ├── docker/
│   │   ├── entrypoint.sh
│   │   └── healthcheck.sh
│   └── Makefile
├── frontend/
│   ├── src/
│   ├── public/
│   ├── package.json
│   ├── package-lock.json
│   ├── nginx/
│   │   ├── nginx.conf
│   │   └── default.conf
│   ├── Dockerfile
│   ├── .dockerignore
│   └── Makefile
├── ci/
│   ├── trivy/
│   │   └── trivy.yaml
│   ├── sbom/
│   └── scripts/
├── docs/
│   └── release-versioning.md
└── gitops/
    └── ...

Why this structure matters

Keeps backend and frontend independently buildable.

Keeps runtime entrypoint logic version-controlled.

Makes health checks explicit and reviewable.

Makes CI integration straightforward.

Makes build context small and intentional.



---

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files


---

1. Django backend Dockerfile

# syntax=docker/dockerfile:1.7

############################
# Builder stage
############################
FROM python:3.12-slim-bookworm AS builder

ENV PYTHONDONTWRITEBYTECODE=1 \
    PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1 \
    PIP_DISABLE_PIP_VERSION_CHECK=1 \
    PIP_NO_CACHE_DIR=1

WORKDIR /build

RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \
    build-essential \
    gcc \
    libpq-dev \
    curl \
    && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

COPY backend/app/requirements/ ./requirements/
RUN python -m venv /opt/venv
ENV PATH="/opt/venv/bin:$PATH"

RUN pip install --upgrade pip setuptools wheel && \
    pip install -r requirements/prod.txt

############################
# Runtime stage
############################
FROM python:3.12-slim-bookworm AS runtime

ENV PYTHONDONTWRITEBYTECODE=1 \
    PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1 \
    PATH="/opt/venv/bin:$PATH" \
    APP_HOME="/app" \
    PORT=8000

WORKDIR ${APP_HOME}

RUN groupadd --system appgroup && \
    useradd --system --gid appgroup --create-home --home-dir /home/appuser appuser

RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \
    libpq5 \
    curl \
    && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

COPY --from=builder /opt/venv /opt/venv
COPY backend/app/ ${APP_HOME}/
COPY backend/docker/entrypoint.sh /entrypoint.sh
COPY backend/docker/healthcheck.sh /healthcheck.sh

RUN chmod +x /entrypoint.sh /healthcheck.sh && \
    chown -R appuser:appgroup ${APP_HOME} /home/appuser

USER appuser

EXPOSE 8000

HEALTHCHECK --interval=30s --timeout=5s --start-period=30s --retries=3 \
  CMD ["/healthcheck.sh"]

ENTRYPOINT ["/entrypoint.sh"]
CMD ["gunicorn", "--config", "gunicorn.conf.py", "project.wsgi:application"]


---

2. Django entrypoint

#!/usr/bin/env sh
set -eu

echo "Starting Django application..."

python manage.py collectstatic --noinput

exec "$@"


---

3. Django healthcheck

#!/usr/bin/env sh
set -eu

curl --fail --silent http://127.0.0.1:8000/healthz || exit 1


---

4. Gunicorn config for Django

bind = "0.0.0.0:8000"
workers = 4
worker_class = "gthread"
threads = 4
timeout = 60
graceful_timeout = 30
keepalive = 5
max_requests = 1000
max_requests_jitter = 100
accesslog = "-"
errorlog = "-"
loglevel = "info"

---

6. React frontend Dockerfile

# syntax=docker/dockerfile:1.7

############################
# Build stage
############################
FROM node:22-bookworm-slim AS builder

WORKDIR /build

COPY frontend/package*.json ./
RUN npm ci

COPY frontend/ ./
RUN npm run build

############################
# Runtime stage
############################
FROM nginx:1.27-alpine AS runtime

RUN addgroup -S appgroup && adduser -S appuser -G appgroup

COPY frontend/nginx/nginx.conf /etc/nginx/nginx.conf
COPY frontend/nginx/default.conf /etc/nginx/conf.d/default.conf
COPY --from=builder /build/build /usr/share/nginx/html

RUN chown -R appuser:appgroup /usr/share/nginx/html /var/cache/nginx /var/run /etc/nginx/conf.d && \
    touch /var/run/nginx.pid && \
    chown appuser:appgroup /var/run/nginx.pid

USER appuser

EXPOSE 8080

HEALTHCHECK --interval=30s --timeout=5s --start-period=20s --retries=3 \
  CMD wget -q -O /dev/null http://127.0.0.1:8080/healthz || exit 1

CMD ["nginx", "-g", "daemon off;"]


---

7. NGINX main config

user  appuser;
worker_processes  auto;

error_log  /dev/stderr warn;
pid        /var/run/nginx.pid;

events {
    worker_connections  1024;
}

http {
    include       /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type  application/octet-stream;

    access_log /dev/stdout;
    sendfile        on;
    keepalive_timeout  65;

    include /etc/nginx/conf.d/*.conf;
}


---

8. NGINX app config

server {
    listen 8080;
    server_name _;

    root /usr/share/nginx/html;
    index index.html;

    location = /healthz {
        access_log off;
        return 200 "ok\n";
        add_header Content-Type text/plain;
    }

    location /static/ {
        expires 7d;
        add_header Cache-Control "public, max-age=604800, immutable";
        try_files $uri =404;
    }

    location / {
        try_files $uri /index.html;
    }
}

10. Example build metadata labels

Add these labels to both Dockerfiles if your CI injects metadata:

LABEL org.opencontainers.image.title="myapp-backend" \
      org.opencontainers.image.description="Django backend service" \
      org.opencontainers.image.source="https://git.example.com/platform/myapp" \
      org.opencontainers.image.vendor="ExampleCorp" \
      org.opencontainers.image.licenses="Proprietary"

CI can inject dynamic values during build:

revision

created timestamp

version

commit SHA

branch

build URL



---

11. Version tagging strategy

Use this format for ECR image tags:

<semver>-<gitsha>

Examples:

backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d
backend:2.8.5-f9e8d7c
frontend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d

Also publish a CI build tag:

backend:build-20260412-154500-a1b2c3d
frontend:build-20260412-154500-a1b2c3d

Recommended promotion model:

human-friendly tag for release visibility

immutable digest for deployment truth


Example deployment reference:

image:
  repository: 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/backend
  tag: "2.8.4-a1b2c3d"
  digest: "sha256:8bd7b0f..."


---

12. Example backend health endpoint in Django

from django.http import JsonResponse

def healthz(request):
    return JsonResponse({"status": "ok"})

Production refinement:

/healthz = cheap liveness

/readyz = dependency-aware readiness

/startupz = optional slow-start signal


Example readiness pattern:

from django.db import connection
from django.http import JsonResponse

def readyz(request):
    try:
        with connection.cursor() as cursor:
            cursor.execute("SELECT 1")
            cursor.fetchone()
        return JsonResponse({"status": "ready"})
    except Exception:
        return JsonResponse({"status": "not-ready"}, status=503)


---

13. Example ECR repository policy settings to align with this layer

This is managed later in Terraform, but the container strategy assumes:

image tag immutability enabled

scan-on-push enabled

lifecycle policy enabled

KMS encryption enabled

cross-region replication enabled for DR



---

F. Line-by-line explanation of critical sections

Backend Dockerfile critical sections

FROM python:3.12-slim-bookworm AS builder

Uses a slim Debian-based Python image. This is a pragmatic enterprise choice.

Why not Alpine?

musl-based edge cases with Python packages

more pain with native dependencies

harder debugging for some libraries

less consistent vendor support patterns


For enterprise Python workloads, slim Debian is often safer than Alpine.


---

python -m venv /opt/venv

Creates an isolated runtime dependency tree.

Why this matters:

avoids polluting system Python

makes builder-to-runtime copy clean

keeps runtime predictable



---

pip install -r requirements/prod.txt

Only production dependencies should enter runtime. No test tools, linters, or dev-only packages.

Enterprise mistake: installing everything from a monolithic requirements.txt, then shipping pytest, mypy, and build tools into prod.


---

Runtime stage separation

Builder and runtime are separated to prevent:

compiler toolchains in final image

unnecessary headers and build packages

attack surface bloat

larger image sizes


This is the core principle of multi-stage hardening.


---

groupadd and useradd

Creates a dedicated non-root user.

Why:

prevents easy privilege escalation inside the container

aligns with Pod Security Standards

works better with admission controls that reject root containers



---

USER appuser

This is non-negotiable for production-grade workloads unless there is a very specific justification.

Auditors and platform security teams look for this immediately.


---

HEALTHCHECK

This provides a container-native health contract.

Important distinction:

Docker HEALTHCHECK helps image/runtime consistency

Kubernetes liveness/readiness/startup probes still remain authoritative in-cluster


Do not rely on only one of the two.


---

ENTRYPOINT + CMD

This pattern lets the image define the startup contract while still allowing runtime argument overrides.

ENTRYPOINT = stable wrapper logic

CMD = default main process



---

Frontend Dockerfile critical sections

FROM node:22-bookworm-slim AS builder

Node is used only to compile assets. It is not present in the runtime image.

That is a major reduction in attack surface.


---

FROM nginx:1.27-alpine AS runtime

Runtime serves static assets only.

Why NGINX:

predictable

fast

simple

easy health checks

cheap operationally



---

USER appuser

NGINX often runs as root in simplistic examples. That is not acceptable in hardened enterprise deployments.

This config adjusts permissions so NGINX can run as non-root on port 8080.


---

React location /

This supports SPA routing by falling back to index.html.

Without it, React routes such as /settings/profile break on refresh.


---

Static caching

Cache-Control: immutable on static assets assumes content-hashed asset names from the React build output.

This significantly reduces repeat load costs and improves frontend performance.


---

Health check strategy

Backend

Use three logical endpoints:

liveness: process alive

readiness: dependencies ready

startup: slow boot tolerance


Example production mapping:

/healthz → liveness

/readyz → readiness

/startupz → startup


Frontend

A static /healthz endpoint is sufficient because NGINX is the runtime service.


---

Version tagging strategy explained

Never use latest

Why it is dangerous:

not deterministic

breaks rollback

hides provenance

makes audit reconstruction harder

can create environment drift


Recommended tags

Each image gets:

semver + git SHA

build timestamp + git SHA

digest recorded by CI


What is actually deployed

Kubernetes should ultimately track either:

immutable tag under immutable ECR policy

or ideally image digest


For highly controlled prod, digest pinning is preferred.


---

Supply chain considerations

This is where containerization becomes enterprise-grade.

Required controls

1. Base image governance

Use approved base images only.

Options:

official minimal images

internally curated golden base images

vendor-signed enterprise bases


Security teams often maintain an allowlist.


---

2. Dependency pinning

Pin Python and Node dependencies.

For Python:

pinned versions in requirements

preferably hashes using pip-compile --generate-hashes


For Node:

commit package-lock.json

use npm ci, not npm install



---

3. Vulnerability scanning

Images are scanned in CI with Trivy before push, and again at registry level with ECR scan-on-push.

This gives:

build-time feedback

registry-time visibility

defense in depth



---

4. SBOM generation

Generate SBOMs in CI using tools like:

Syft

Trivy SBOM mode


Store SBOM artifacts with the build and optionally attach as OCI artifact or CI artifact.

Why:

required for modern supply-chain visibility

useful during zero-day response

increasingly expected in compliance and customer assurance reviews



---

5. Image signing

Strong enterprise recommendation:

sign images with Cosign

verify signatures in admission policy later


Even if not enforced on day one, design for it now.


---

6. Secret-free builds

Never bake secrets into the image.

No:

.env copied into image

API keys in frontend bundle

credentials in Docker build args


Use:

AWS Secrets Manager

External Secrets / CSI pattern later

runtime injection only



---

7. OCI metadata labels

OCI labels improve auditability and provenance tracking.

Track:

source repo

commit SHA

build timestamp

version

owner/team



---

G. Commands to run

Backend local build

docker build -f backend/Dockerfile -t myapp-backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d .

What it does:

reads the backend Dockerfile

executes multi-stage build

creates a local image tagged myapp-backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d



---

Frontend local build

docker build -f frontend/Dockerfile -t myapp-frontend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d .

What it does:

builds React static assets

packages them into hardened NGINX image

creates a local image with immutable-style tag



---

Run backend locally

docker run --rm -p 8000:8000 myapp-backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d

What it does:

starts the Django container

exposes app on localhost port 8000

removes container after exit



---

Run frontend locally

docker run --rm -p 8080:8080 myapp-frontend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d

What it does:

starts NGINX serving the React build

exposes app on localhost port 8080



---

Check backend health

curl -i http://localhost:8000/healthz

What it does:

calls the backend health endpoint

verifies the container is serving traffic correctly



---

Check frontend health

curl -i http://localhost:8080/healthz

What it does:

calls the NGINX health endpoint

confirms the frontend runtime is healthy



---

Tag for ECR

docker tag myapp-backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d
docker tag myapp-frontend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/frontend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d

What it does:

creates ECR-compatible tags for push



---

Authenticate to ECR

aws ecr get-login-password --region us-east-1 \
  | docker login --username AWS --password-stdin 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com

What it does:

gets temporary registry auth token from AWS

logs Docker into the target ECR registry



---

Push images to ECR

docker push 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d
docker push 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/frontend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d

What it does:

uploads the built artifacts into ECR

those tags remain immutable once ECR immutability is enabled



---

Inspect image digest after push

aws ecr describe-images \
  --repository-name myapp/backend \
  --region us-east-1 \
  --image-ids imageTag=2.8.4-a1b2c3d

What it does:

retrieves image metadata

returns the immutable digest that Kubernetes/GitOps can reference



---

Generate SBOM with Syft

syft 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d -o spdx-json > backend-sbom.json

What it does:

enumerates packages in the image

writes an SBOM in SPDX JSON format



---

Scan image with Trivy

trivy image --severity HIGH,CRITICAL --exit-code 1 \
  123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/backend:2.8.4-a1b2c3d

What it does:

scans the image for vulnerabilities

fails the command if high/critical issues are found



---

Production pull by digest example

docker pull 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/backend@sha256:8bd7b0f...

What it does:

pulls the exact immutable artifact

avoids ambiguity of tags entirely



---

H. Production best practices

1. Use minimal but supportable base images

Prefer slim Debian or approved hardened enterprise bases over convenience-heavy images.


---

2. Run as non-root always

This should be enforced at both image layer and Kubernetes admission layer.


---

3. Keep runtime images free of build tools

No compiler, no package manager caches, no test binaries.


---

4. Separate liveness and readiness logic

Readiness should validate dependencies required for serving traffic.


---

5. Use immutable version tags

Tags should be unique per build and never reused.


---

6. Deploy by digest for prod

Even with ECR immutability, digest pinning gives the strongest audit trail.


---

7. Scan before push and after push

CI scan plus ECR scan-on-push is the right enterprise pattern.


---

8. Generate and retain SBOMs

Store them with build artifacts and retain them according to audit policy.


---

9. Keep secrets out of images entirely

No secrets in Dockerfiles, build args, static bundles, or repo.


---

10. Add OCI metadata labels

Make artifacts traceable to source, version, and ownership.


---

11. Tune startup and worker model intentionally

Gunicorn defaults are rarely right for production. Set workers and threads based on CPU profile, latency characteristics, and pod sizing.


---

12. Prefer frontend static delivery separation

React should build once, serve statically, and avoid embedding mutable runtime secrets. Public configuration should be minimal and intentional.


---

13. Prepare for signature verification

Even if Cosign verification is not active on day one, design the artifact flow to support it.


---

I. Common enterprise mistakes

1. Using latest

This destroys deterministic rollback and environment traceability.


---

2. Shipping root containers

Very common in immature environments. Immediate red flag in security review.


---

3. Baking secrets into image or JS bundle

Especially dangerous for frontend apps where anything in the bundle is effectively public.


---

4. Single-stage Dockerfiles

These often ship compilers, headers, package caches, and dev dependencies into prod.


---

5. No .dockerignore

This causes slow builds, secret leakage risk, and huge build contexts.


---

6. Treating health checks as optional

Without them, Kubernetes and load balancers cannot make safe traffic decisions.


---

7. Unpinned dependencies

Rebuilding the same commit days later may produce a different artifact.


---

8. No SBOM

This makes zero-day response slow and uncertain.


---

9. Oversized images

Slower pulls increase deploy time and increase failure window during scaling or failover.


---

10. Using Alpine blindly for Python

Can work, but often causes more enterprise support pain than benefit for complex Python stacks.


---

11. Letting frontend runtime require root for port 80

Use non-root and a higher unprivileged port like 8080.


---

12. Not preserving provenance

If you cannot answer “which commit produced this image digest?” in seconds, the artifact pipeline is not enterprise-grade.


---

J. Interview explanation (Senior/Principal level)

A strong Principal-level explanation would sound like this:

> I treat containerization as the first production control plane, not just packaging. My design uses multi-stage Dockerfiles, non-root execution, minimal runtime images, explicit health checks, immutable version tags, and artifact provenance labels. Images are pushed to ECR with tag immutability enabled and later referenced by digest in GitOps manifests. I also require pre-push vulnerability scanning, SBOM generation, and a path to signature verification. The key architectural goal is to make every deployment artifact reproducible, auditable, secure by default, and safe to roll forward or backward without ambiguity.



Then go deeper:

Why this is Principal-level

Because the design connects container build choices to:

deployment safety

compliance evidence

failover readiness

rollback integrity

runtime hardening

cost and latency during scaling events


Design trade-off examples

Python slim vs Alpine

I would explain that Alpine is smaller, but slim Debian is often the better enterprise choice for Python because native dependency support and operational consistency matter more than saving a few MB.

Tag vs digest

Tags are useful for humans, but digests are the real deployment truth. In prod, I prefer digest-pinned releases.

Static frontend in NGINX vs Node server

Unless there is SSR or edge runtime complexity, static NGINX serving is simpler, cheaper, and safer.

Why image immutability matters

Without immutability, a rollback tag can silently point to a different binary over time, which breaks both audit and incident recovery.

What interviewers want to hear

They want to hear that you understand containerization as part of:

software supply-chain security

deployment reliability

platform governance

artifact lifecycle management


Not just “I wrote a Dockerfile.”

                    +-----------------------------------------------+


---

Layer 1 outcome

After this layer is implemented correctly, you have:

secure container artifacts

immutable image versioning

ECR-ready packaging

health-check-capable runtimes

CI scanability

SBOM-ready artifacts

a foundation for GitOps, policy enforcement, and zero-downtime rollout

2. Infrastructure Layer (Terraform)

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer provisions the cloud foundation for the platform using Terraform as the infrastructure-as-code engine.

It is responsible for creating and managing:

Terraform remote state in S3 + DynamoDB

Multi-environment layout: dev, stage, prod

Primary and secondary AWS regions

VPC networking with:

public subnets

private application/worker subnets

private database subnets


Internet Gateway + NAT Gateways

Amazon EKS with private worker nodes

Amazon RDS Multi-AZ

Amazon ECR with immutability and scan-on-push

IAM OIDC provider for IRSA

EKS control plane logging

Cross-region disaster recovery foundations

KMS-backed encryption defaults

Audit-friendly tagging and structure


Terraform in this design is not just “how infra is created.” It is the authoritative control plane for platform state, the change history for cloud architecture, and the compliance evidence source for infrastructure decisions.


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

After containerization, the next requirement is a reproducible, reviewable, multi-environment infrastructure foundation.

Without this layer, platform teams face:

manual drift

inconsistent environments

untraceable changes

fragile networking

insecure IAM patterns

environment snowflakes

weak disaster recovery posture


At enterprise scale, Terraform is needed here because it gives:

repeatability

change review

modularity

promotion discipline

environment parity

fast recovery

auditability


This is the layer that makes the rest of the system possible:

Kubernetes runs on EKS created here

RDS is created here

ECR is created here

OIDC/IRSA enable secure pod-to-AWS access later

VPC/subnets/security boundaries created here determine whether the runtime is actually private and secure



---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

A Principal-level design treats infrastructure as a productized platform, not a collection of AWS resources.

1. Environment separation is mandatory

dev, stage, and prod must not merely be namespace-separated in Kubernetes. At enterprise scale, they should have meaningful infrastructure separation, typically at least by account or by isolated state/workspaces and often by separate VPCs.

A realistic model is:

separate AWS accounts per environment

separate Terraform states per region/environment

reusable module library

environment-specific composition layer


That reduces blast radius and improves access governance.


---

2. Network boundaries must reflect trust boundaries

Private nodes mean:

EKS worker nodes live in private subnets

no direct public IPs on nodes

public ingress terminates through ALB in public subnets

database lives in isolated DB subnets

worker-to-database path remains private


This is a core “secure by default” pattern.


---

3. RDS belongs outside the cluster

The database is stateful, operationally sensitive, and should be managed through AWS RDS, not self-hosted inside Kubernetes. This aligns with the requirement and is the correct enterprise choice for:

patching

backups

failover

durability

observability integration

separation of duties



---

4. Remote state must be durable and lockable

Using S3 + DynamoDB provides:

durability of state

version history

concurrency locking

safer CI execution

disaster recovery for IaC metadata


Without a proper backend, Terraform becomes unreliable in teams.


---

5. Multi-region must be designed, not retrofitted

A secondary region is not just “create the same resources elsewhere someday.”

It requires early design for:

VPC address space planning

image availability in regional ECR

replicated secrets strategy

RDS cross-region replication

Route53 failover

environment topology symmetry

state separation per region



---

6. OIDC/IRSA is foundational

Traditional node IAM roles cause over-permissioning because every pod on a node can inherit that privilege path indirectly. OIDC + IRSA lets us bind IAM to Kubernetes service accounts, giving least-privilege access to specific workloads.

That is a cornerstone security control and should be enabled from day one.


---

7. Logging at the control plane is not optional

EKS control plane logs support:

incident response

access review

audit evidence

troubleshooting auth failures

API misuse investigation


At enterprise scale, missing audit/authenticator logs is a major weakness.


---

8. Tagging and module design matter for cost and governance

A strong Terraform implementation makes every resource attributable by:

application

environment

owner

cost center

compliance tier

data classification


That drives both cost reporting and auditability.


---

D. Folder structure

A realistic enterprise Terraform repository structure:

infra/
├── README.md
├── versions.tf
├── providers.tf
├── backend-bootstrap/
│   ├── main.tf
│   ├── variables.tf
│   ├── outputs.tf
│   └── terraform.tfvars.example
├── modules/
│   ├── vpc/
│   │   ├── main.tf
│   │   ├── variables.tf
│   │   ├── outputs.tf
│   │   └── README.md
│   ├── ecr/
│   │   ├── main.tf
│   │   ├── variables.tf
│   │   └── outputs.tf
│   ├── eks/
│   │   ├── main.tf
│   │   ├── iam.tf
│   │   ├── logging.tf
│   │   ├── nodegroups.tf
│   │   ├── oidc.tf
│   │   ├── variables.tf
│   │   └── outputs.tf
│   ├── rds-postgres/
│   │   ├── main.tf
│   │   ├── security.tf
│   │   ├── subnet-group.tf
│   │   ├── parameter-group.tf
│   │   ├── variables.tf
│   │   └── outputs.tf
│   ├── kms/
│   │   ├── main.tf
│   │   ├── variables.tf
│   │   └── outputs.tf
│   ├── route53-failover/
│   │   ├── main.tf
│   │   ├── variables.tf
│   │   └── outputs.tf
│   └── tags/
│       ├── locals.tf
│       └── outputs.tf
├── live/
│   ├── dev/
│   │   ├── us-east-1/
│   │   │   ├── main.tf
│   │   │   ├── backend.tf
│   │   │   ├── variables.tf
│   │   │   ├── terraform.tfvars
│   │   │   └── outputs.tf
│   │   └── us-west-2/
│   │       ├── main.tf
│   │       ├── backend.tf
│   │       ├── variables.tf
│   │       ├── terraform.tfvars
│   │       └── outputs.tf
│   ├── stage/
│   │   ├── us-east-1/
│   │   └── us-west-2/
│   └── prod/
│       ├── us-east-1/
│       └── us-west-2/
└── policies/
    ├── tfsec-baseline.yaml
    └── checkov-baseline.yaml

Why this structure is enterprise-grade

modules/

Reusable building blocks with opinionated defaults.

live/

Environment and region composition layer. This is what platform teams actually apply.

backend-bootstrap/

Used only once or rarely to create the Terraform backend resources themselves, because Terraform cannot use an S3 backend that does not yet exist.

policies/

Static IaC policy and security scanning configuration.

This separation keeps the codebase maintainable for multiple teams and regions.

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files

1. Terraform version and provider baseline

infra/versions.tf

terraform {
  required_version = "~> 1.8.0"

  required_providers {
    aws = {
      source  = "hashicorp/aws"
      version = "~> 5.50"
    }
  }
}


---

infra/providers.tf

provider "aws" {
  region = var.aws_region

  default_tags {
    tags = local.common_tags
  }
}

provider "aws" {
  alias  = "secondary"
  region = var.secondary_region

  default_tags {
    tags = local.common_tags
  }
}


---

2. Remote state bootstrap

infra/backend-bootstrap/main.tf

terraform {
  required_version = "~> 1.8.0"

  required_providers {
    aws = {
      source  = "hashicorp/aws"
      version = "~> 5.50"
    }
  }
}

provider "aws" {
  region = var.region
}

resource "aws_s3_bucket" "tf_state" {
  bucket = var.state_bucket_name
}

resource "aws_s3_bucket_versioning" "tf_state" {
  bucket = aws_s3_bucket.tf_state.id

  versioning_configuration {
    status = "Enabled"
  }
}

resource "aws_s3_bucket_server_side_encryption_configuration" "tf_state" {
  bucket = aws_s3_bucket.tf_state.id

  rule {
    apply_server_side_encryption_by_default {
      sse_algorithm = "aws:kms"
      kms_master_key_id = aws_kms_key.tf_state.arn
    }
  }
}

resource "aws_s3_bucket_public_access_block" "tf_state" {
  bucket                  = aws_s3_bucket.tf_state.id
  block_public_acls       = true
  block_public_policy     = true
  ignore_public_acls      = true
  restrict_public_buckets = true
}

resource "aws_kms_key" "tf_state" {
  description             = "KMS key for Terraform remote state"
  deletion_window_in_days = 30
  enable_key_rotation     = true
}

resource "aws_dynamodb_table" "tf_locks" {
  name         = var.lock_table_name
  billing_mode = "PAY_PER_REQUEST"
  hash_key     = "LockID"

  attribute {
    name = "LockID"
    type = "S"
  }

  point_in_time_recovery {
    enabled = true
  }
}


---

infra/backend-bootstrap/variables.tf

variable "region" {
  type        = string
  description = "AWS region for backend resources"
}

variable "state_bucket_name" {
  type        = string
  description = "S3 bucket name for Terraform state"
}

variable "lock_table_name" {
  type        = string
  description = "DynamoDB table name for Terraform locking"
}


---

infra/live/prod/us-east-1/backend.tf

terraform {
  backend "s3" {
    bucket         = "examplecorp-prod-terraform-state"
    key            = "prod/us-east-1/platform/terraform.tfstate"
    region         = "us-east-1"
    dynamodb_table = "examplecorp-prod-terraform-locks"
    encrypt        = true
  }
}

locals {

    Owner             = var.owner
    CostCenter        = var.cost_center
    ComplianceScope   = var.compliance_scope
    DataClassification = var.data_classification
    TerraformModule   = var.terraform_module

}

4. VPC module

infra/modules/vpc/variables.tf

variable "name" {
  type = string
}

variable "vpc_cidr" {
  type = string
}

variable "azs" {
  type = list(string)
}

variable "public_subnets" {
  type = list(string)
}

variable "private_app_subnets" {
  type = list(string)
}

variable "private_db_subnets" {
  type = list(string)
}

variable "enable_nat_gateway" {
  type    = bool
  default = true
}

variable "single_nat_gateway" {
  type    = bool
  default = false
}


---

infra/modules/vpc/main.tf

resource "aws_vpc" "this" {
  cidr_block           = var.vpc_cidr
  enable_dns_support   = true
  enable_dns_hostnames = true

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-vpc"
  }
}

resource "aws_internet_gateway" "this" {
  vpc_id = aws_vpc.this.id

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-igw"
  }
}

resource "aws_subnet" "public" {
  for_each = {
    for idx, cidr in var.public_subnets : idx => cidr
  }

  vpc_id                  = aws_vpc.this.id
  cidr_block              = each.value
  availability_zone       = var.azs[tonumber(each.key)]
  map_public_ip_on_launch = false

  tags = {
    Name                                        = "${var.name}-public-${var.azs[tonumber(each.key)]}"
    "kubernetes.io/role/elb"                    = "1"
  }
}

resource "aws_subnet" "private_app" {
  for_each = {
    for idx, cidr in var.private_app_subnets : idx => cidr
  }

  vpc_id            = aws_vpc.this.id
  cidr_block        = each.value
  availability_zone = var.azs[tonumber(each.key)]

  tags = {
    Name                                           = "${var.name}-private-app-${var.azs[tonumber(each.key)]}"
    "kubernetes.io/role/internal-elb"              = "1"
    "karpenter.sh/discovery"                       = var.name
  }
}

resource "aws_subnet" "private_db" {
  for_each = {
    for idx, cidr in var.private_db_subnets : idx => cidr
  }

  vpc_id            = aws_vpc.this.id
  cidr_block        = each.value
  availability_zone = var.azs[tonumber(each.key)]

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-private-db-${var.azs[tonumber(each.key)]}"
  }
}

resource "aws_eip" "nat" {
  count  = var.enable_nat_gateway ? (var.single_nat_gateway ? 1 : length(var.public_subnets)) : 0
  domain = "vpc"

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-nat-eip-${count.index}"
  }
}

resource "aws_nat_gateway" "this" {
  count         = var.enable_nat_gateway ? (var.single_nat_gateway ? 1 : length(var.public_subnets)) : 0
  allocation_id = aws_eip.nat[count.index].id
  subnet_id     = aws_subnet.public[var.single_nat_gateway ? 0 : count.index].id

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-nat-${count.index}"
  }

  depends_on = [aws_internet_gateway.this]
}

resource "aws_route_table" "public" {
  vpc_id = aws_vpc.this.id

  route {
    cidr_block = "0.0.0.0/0"
    gateway_id = aws_internet_gateway.this.id
  }

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-public-rt"
  }
}

resource "aws_route_table_association" "public" {
  for_each = aws_subnet.public

  subnet_id      = each.value.id
  route_table_id = aws_route_table.public.id
}

resource "aws_route_table" "private_app" {
  count  = length(var.private_app_subnets)
  vpc_id = aws_vpc.this.id

  route {
    cidr_block     = "0.0.0.0/0"
    nat_gateway_id = aws_nat_gateway.this[var.single_nat_gateway ? 0 : count.index].id
  }

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-private-app-rt-${count.index}"
  }
}

resource "aws_route_table_association" "private_app" {
  for_each = aws_subnet.private_app

  subnet_id      = each.value.id
  route_table_id = aws_route_table.private_app[tonumber(each.key)].id
}

resource "aws_route_table" "private_db" {
  count  = length(var.private_db_subnets)
  vpc_id = aws_vpc.this.id

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-private-db-rt-${count.index}"
  }
}

resource "aws_route_table_association" "private_db" {
  for_each = aws_subnet.private_db

  subnet_id      = each.value.id
  route_table_id = aws_route_table.private_db[tonumber(each.key)].id
}


---

infra/modules/vpc/outputs.tf

output "vpc_id" {
  value = aws_vpc.this.id
}

output "public_subnet_ids" {
  value = [for s in aws_subnet.public : s.id]
}

output "private_app_subnet_ids" {
  value = [for s in aws_subnet.private_app : s.id]
}

output "private_db_subnet_ids" {
  value = [for s in aws_subnet.private_db : s.id]
}

5. ECR module

infra/modules/ecr/main.tf

resource "aws_ecr_repository" "this" {
  name                 = var.name
  image_tag_mutability = "IMMUTABLE"

  image_scanning_configuration {
    scan_on_push = true
  }

  encryption_configuration {
    encryption_type = "KMS"
    kms_key         = var.kms_key_arn
  }

  tags = {
    Name = var.name
  }
}

resource "aws_ecr_lifecycle_policy" "this" {
  repository = aws_ecr_repository.this.name

  policy = jsonencode({
    rules = [
      {
        rulePriority = 1
        description  = "Expire untagged images after 7 days"
        selection = {
          tagStatus   = "untagged"
          countType   = "sinceImagePushed"
          countUnit   = "days"
          countNumber = 7
        }
        action = {
          type = "expire"
        }
      },
      {
        rulePriority = 2
        description  = "Retain last 200 tagged images"
        selection = {
          tagStatus     = "tagged"
          tagPrefixList = ["build-", "v"]
          countType     = "imageCountMoreThan"
          countNumber   = 200
        }
        action = {
          type = "expire"
        }
      }
    ]
  })
}


---

infra/modules/ecr/variables.tf

variable "name" {
  type = string
}

variable "kms_key_arn" {
  type = string
}


---

infra/modules/ecr/outputs.tf

output "repository_url" {
  value = aws_ecr_repository.this.repository_url
}

output "repository_arn" {
  value = aws_ecr_repository.this.arn
}

  default = false

  type    = bool
  default = true

  type = string

  type = list(string)

  type = list(string)

  type = number

infra/modules/eks/main.tf

resource "aws_iam_role" "cluster" {
  name = "${var.cluster_name}-cluster-role"

  assume_role_policy = jsonencode({
    Version = "2012-10-17"
    Statement = [{
      Effect = "Allow"
      Principal = {
        Service = "eks.amazonaws.com"
      }
      Action = "sts:AssumeRole"
    }]
  })
}

resource "aws_iam_role_policy_attachment" "cluster_policy" {
  role       = aws_iam_role.cluster.name
  policy_arn = "arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/AmazonEKSClusterPolicy"
}

resource "aws_cloudwatch_log_group" "eks" {
  name              = "/aws/eks/${var.cluster_name}/control-plane"
  retention_in_days = 30
}

resource "aws_eks_cluster" "this" {
  name     = var.cluster_name
  role_arn = aws_iam_role.cluster.arn
  version  = var.cluster_version

  enabled_cluster_log_types = var.enabled_cluster_log_types

  vpc_config {
    subnet_ids              = var.private_subnet_ids
    endpoint_private_access = var.endpoint_private_access
    endpoint_public_access  = var.endpoint_public_access
  }

  encryption_config {
    provider {
      key_arn = var.eks_kms_key_arn
    }
    resources = ["secrets"]
  }

  depends_on = [
    aws_iam_role_policy_attachment.cluster_policy,
    aws_cloudwatch_log_group.eks
  ]
}


---

infra/modules/eks/nodegroups.tf

resource "aws_iam_role" "node" {
  name = "${var.cluster_name}-node-role"

  assume_role_policy = jsonencode({
    Version = "2012-10-17"
    Statement = [{
      Effect = "Allow"
      Principal = {
        Service = "ec2.amazonaws.com"
      }
      Action = "sts:AssumeRole"
    }]
  })
}

resource "aws_iam_role_policy_attachment" "worker_node_policy" {
  role       = aws_iam_role.node.name
  policy_arn = "arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/AmazonEKSWorkerNodePolicy"
}

resource "aws_iam_role_policy_attachment" "cni_policy" {
  role       = aws_iam_role.node.name
  policy_arn = "arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/AmazonEKS_CNI_Policy"
}

resource "aws_iam_role_policy_attachment" "ecr_readonly" {
  role       = aws_iam_role.node.name
  policy_arn = "arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/AmazonEC2ContainerRegistryReadOnly"
}

resource "aws_eks_node_group" "general" {
  cluster_name    = aws_eks_cluster.this.name
  node_group_name = "${var.cluster_name}-general"
  node_role_arn   = aws_iam_role.node.arn
  subnet_ids      = var.private_subnet_ids

  ami_type        = "AL2023_x86_64_STANDARD"
  capacity_type   = "ON_DEMAND"
  instance_types  = var.node_instance_types

  scaling_config {
    desired_size = var.node_desired_size
    min_size     = var.node_min_size
    max_size     = var.node_max_size
  }

  update_config {
    max_unavailable_percentage = 25
  }

  labels = {
    workload = "general"
  }

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.cluster_name}-general-ng"
    "k8s.io/cluster-autoscaler/enabled"                 = "true"
    "k8s.io/cluster-autoscaler/${var.cluster_name}"     = "owned"
  }

  depends_on = [
    aws_iam_role_policy_attachment.worker_node_policy,
    aws_iam_role_policy_attachment.cni_policy,
    aws_iam_role_policy_attachment.ecr_readonly
  ]
}


---

infra/modules/eks/oidc.tf

data "tls_certificate" "this" {
  url = aws_eks_cluster.this.identity[0].oidc[0].issuer
}

resource "aws_iam_openid_connect_provider" "this" {
  client_id_list  = ["sts.amazonaws.com"]
  thumbprint_list = [data.tls_certificate.this.certificates[0].sha1_fingerprint]
  url             = aws_eks_cluster.this.identity[0].oidc[0].issuer
}

}

resource "aws_security_group" "db" {
  name        = "${var.identifier}-db-sg"
  description = "RDS security group"
  vpc_id      = var.vpc_id

  ingress {
    description = "Postgres from private app subnets"
    from_port   = 5432
    to_port     = 5432
    protocol    = "tcp"
    cidr_blocks = var.app_subnet_cidr_blocks
  }

  egress {
    from_port   = 0
    to_port     = 0
    protocol    = "-1"
    cidr_blocks = ["0.0.0.0/0"]
  }
}

resource "aws_db_parameter_group" "this" {
  name   = "${var.identifier}-pg"
  family = "postgres16"

  parameter {
    name  = "log_connections"
    value = "1"
  }

  parameter {
    name  = "log_disconnections"
    value = "1"
  }

  parameter {
    name  = "log_min_duration_statement"
    value = "1000"
  }
}


---

infra/modules/rds-postgres/main.tf

resource "aws_db_instance" "this" {
  identifier                     = var.identifier
  engine                         = "postgres"
  engine_version                 = var.engine_version
  instance_class                 = var.instance_class
  allocated_storage              = var.allocated_storage
  max_allocated_storage          = var.max_allocated_storage
  db_name                        = var.db_name
  username                       = var.username
  password                       = var.password
  db_subnet_group_name           = aws_db_subnet_group.this.name
  vpc_security_group_ids         = [aws_security_group.db.id]
  parameter_group_name           = aws_db_parameter_group.this.name
  storage_encrypted              = true
  kms_key_id                     = var.kms_key_arn
  multi_az                       = var.multi_az
  backup_retention_period        = var.backup_retention_period
  backup_window                  = "03:00-04:00"
  maintenance_window             = "sun:04:00-sun:05:00"
  deletion_protection            = true
  skip_final_snapshot            = false
  final_snapshot_identifier      = "${var.identifier}-final-snapshot"
  auto_minor_version_upgrade     = true
  performance_insights_enabled   = true
  publicly_accessible            = false
  copy_tags_to_snapshot          = true
  enabled_cloudwatch_logs_exports = ["postgresql"]

  lifecycle {
    prevent_destroy = true
  }
}

  value = aws_db_instance.this.address
}

  value = aws_db_instance.this.arn

  value = aws_security_group.db.id
}

9. Route53 failover module

infra/modules/route53-failover/main.tf

resource "aws_route53_health_check" "primary" {
  fqdn                            = var.primary_alb_dns_name
  port                            = 443
  type                            = "HTTPS"
  resource_path                   = "/healthz"
  failure_threshold               = 3
  request_interval                = 30
  cloudwatch_alarm_region         = var.primary_region
  insufficient_data_health_status = "Failure"
}

resource "aws_route53_record" "primary" {
  zone_id = var.zone_id
  name    = var.record_name
  type    = "A"

  failover_routing_policy {
    type = "PRIMARY"
  }

  set_identifier = "primary"
  health_check_id = aws_route53_health_check.primary.id

  alias {
    name                   = var.primary_alb_dns_name
    zone_id                = var.primary_alb_zone_id
    evaluate_target_health = true
  }
}

resource "aws_route53_record" "secondary" {
  zone_id = var.zone_id
  name    = var.record_name
  type    = "A"

  failover_routing_policy {
    type = "SECONDARY"
  }

  set_identifier = "secondary"

  alias {
    name                   = var.secondary_alb_dns_name
    zone_id                = var.secondary_alb_zone_id
    evaluate_target_health = true
  }
}

  public_subnets      = ["10.10.0.0/24", "10.10.1.0/24", "10.10.2.0/24"]

  private_db_subnets  = ["10.10.20.0/24", "10.10.21.0/24", "10.10.22.0/24"]

12. Cross-region RDS replica example

For DR topology, the primary region hosts the writable RDS instance, and the secondary region hosts a cross-region read replica or promoted DR instance pattern depending on engine support and design choice.

Example addition in secondary region

resource "aws_db_instance" "cross_region_replica" {
  provider                    = aws
  identifier                  = "fullstack-platform-prod-us-west-2-postgres-replica"
  replicate_source_db         = "arn:aws:rds:us-east-1:123456789012:db:fullstack-platform-prod-us-east-1-postgres"
  instance_class              = "db.r6g.large"
  publicly_accessible         = false
  storage_encrypted           = true
  kms_key_id                  = module.kms_eks.key_arn
  auto_minor_version_upgrade  = true
  backup_retention_period     = 7
  skip_final_snapshot         = false
}

This is illustrative. In a real platform, you would decide between:

cross-region read replica

Aurora Global Database if the product requirements justify it

snapshot copy plus restore strategy for lower-cost DR


Given the user’s constraint says managed RDS and not DB in cluster, the core requirement is satisfied. The DR section later will formalize the operational pattern.


---

F. Line-by-line explanation of critical sections

Terraform remote state

S3 state bucket

resource "aws_s3_bucket" "tf_state" {
  bucket = var.state_bucket_name
}

This creates the durable storage location for Terraform state.

Why it matters:

state is the map of real resources to code

if state is lost or corrupted, Terraform operations become unsafe

S3 is durable and operationally simple



---

Versioning enabled

resource "aws_s3_bucket_versioning" "tf_state" {
  bucket = aws_s3_bucket.tf_state.id

  versioning_configuration {
    status = "Enabled"
  }
}

This preserves prior versions of state files.

Why:

protects against accidental overwrite

helps recover from CI mistakes

critical for audit reconstruction and rollback of infrastructure metadata



---

SSE-KMS encryption

resource "aws_s3_bucket_server_side_encryption_configuration" "tf_state"

State files often contain sensitive data or references. Encrypting them with KMS is required in serious environments.


---

Public access block

resource "aws_s3_bucket_public_access_block" "tf_state"

This prevents accidental exposure of state data. This is a basic but essential security control.


---

DynamoDB lock table

resource "aws_dynamodb_table" "tf_locks"

Terraform is not safe for team use without locking.

Why:

prevents concurrent apply corruption

protects from overlapping CI pipelines

avoids state race conditions



---

VPC design

enable_dns_support and enable_dns_hostnames

These are required for sane AWS service resolution and internal service behavior, especially for EKS nodes and private service access.


---

Public subnets

resource "aws_subnet" "public"

These host internet-facing infrastructure such as:

ALBs

NAT gateways


They are not for worker nodes.

That is an important enterprise distinction.


---

map_public_ip_on_launch = false

Even in public subnets, this default reduces accidental public IP assignment.

This is a strong secure-by-default pattern.


---

Private app subnets

These are used by:

EKS worker nodes

internal application services

internal load balancers


This satisfies the constraint: worker nodes live in private subnets.


---

Private DB subnets

These isolate database resources from direct ingress and separate database trust boundaries from application compute.

In enterprise reviews, keeping DB subnets distinct is a strong signal of mature network design.


---

NAT gateway per AZ

single_nat_gateway = false

For production, multi-AZ private nodes should not depend on a single NAT gateway.

Why:

reduces AZ-level dependency

preserves egress during partial AZ failure

avoids a hidden single point of failure


Trade-off:

more expensive than single NAT

worth it for production


For lower environments, a single NAT gateway may be acceptable.


---

Separate route tables

Public, private app, and private DB route tables are separated intentionally.

This allows:

different egress models

future VPC endpoints

tighter routing governance

better network troubleshooting



---

ECR design

image_tag_mutability = "IMMUTABLE"

This is one of the most important settings in the whole platform.

Why:

prevents retagging attacks or mistakes

preserves rollback integrity

ensures audit traceability

aligns with versioned image tags and GitOps correctness



---

scan_on_push = true

Registry-side vulnerability scanning adds a second line of defense after CI scanning.

This is not a replacement for CI scanning, but a useful reinforcement.


---

KMS encryption

ECR encryption is standard enterprise posture, particularly when repositories are centrally governed.


---

Lifecycle policy

Keeps the registry from growing forever.

Why:

reduces storage cost

keeps repo hygiene

avoids unbounded artifact sprawl


Important nuance: retain enough release history for rollback and forensic needs.


---

EKS design

Cluster role and policies

resource "aws_iam_role" "cluster"

The control plane needs an IAM role to interact with AWS APIs.

The design attaches only the required managed policy, rather than broad custom admin access.


---

Control plane log group

resource "aws_cloudwatch_log_group" "eks"

A separate explicit log group lets us control retention and logging governance.

This is better than passively accepting defaults.


---

enabled_cluster_log_types

["api", "audit", "authenticator", "controllerManager", "scheduler"]

These logs are essential.

api: API requests and general cluster interaction

audit: security and compliance signal

authenticator: IAM/RBAC auth debugging

controllerManager: control plane controller behavior

scheduler: placement troubleshooting


Audit and authenticator logs are especially valuable during incident response.


---

Private endpoint only

endpoint_public_access  = false
endpoint_private_access = true

This is a hardened production posture.

Why:

the API server is not exposed publicly

cluster administration happens through approved network paths, VPN, bastionless SSM workflows, or CI runners in-network


Trade-off:

requires disciplined operator access design

worth it for strong network posture



---

Secrets encryption

encryption_config {
  provider {
    key_arn = var.eks_kms_key_arn
  }
  resources = ["secrets"]
}

This encrypts Kubernetes secrets in etcd using a customer-managed KMS key.

Even though later we will use AWS Secrets Manager and External Secrets patterns, this still matters because some cluster-native secrets still exist.


---

Managed node group

Private subnet placement

subnet_ids = var.private_subnet_ids

This directly enforces the requirement that nodes are private.


---

AL2023 AMI

ami_type = "AL2023_x86_64_STANDARD"

A modern managed EKS worker base. Cleaner and better aligned with current enterprise Linux expectations than older node images.


---

On-demand baseline

For production, the baseline node group is on-demand for reliability. Spot is introduced selectively later for non-prod or fault-tolerant workloads.


---

Autoscaler tags

"k8s.io/cluster-autoscaler/enabled" = "true"

These tags allow Cluster Autoscaler discovery if that path is used. If Karpenter is used later, discovery tags still remain useful depending on the implementation.


---

max_unavailable_percentage = 25

This improves managed node group rolling updates without replacing all nodes at once.

Important: this must later align with workload PDBs and deployment strategies.


---

OIDC provider for IRSA

TLS certificate data

data "tls_certificate" "this"

Terraform resolves the OIDC provider certificate fingerprint for AWS IAM trust setup.


---

OpenID provider

resource "aws_iam_openid_connect_provider" "this"

This is the AWS-side trust anchor enabling IAM Roles for Service Accounts.

Without this, IRSA cannot work.

This is one of the most critical enablers of least-privilege pod AWS access.


---

RDS design

DB subnet group

Ensures the RDS instance is placed only into the dedicated private DB subnets.


---

DB security group

ingress {
  from_port   = 5432
  ...
  cidr_blocks = var.app_subnet_cidr_blocks
}

This allows only application subnet ranges to connect.

Production refinement: In mature environments, I would often prefer security group to security group rules instead of CIDR-based ingress, because it is more explicit and less brittle. The example is easy to understand, but SG-reference is stronger.


---

storage_encrypted = true

Required for security and compliance baseline.


---

multi_az = true

This is the core HA control for the managed database in the primary region.

It protects against AZ loss and certain infrastructure failures.

It does not replace multi-region DR.


---

backup_retention_period = 14

Provides point-in-time recovery history.

The exact value depends on business requirements and compliance, but 14 days is a credible production baseline.


---

deletion_protection = true

This is a critical control.

It prevents accidental destruction in routine automation or engineer mistakes.


---

prevent_destroy = true

Terraform-side guardrail. This is the IaC equivalent of “slow down before deleting the database.”

A serious enterprise platform should use both infrastructure and Terraform safeguards.


---

performance_insights_enabled = true

Useful for database observability and performance troubleshooting.

This is not just a convenience. At scale it materially reduces MTTR for database-related incidents.


---

publicly_accessible = false

Absolutely required here. The DB must not have a public interface.


---

Secondary region pattern

The secondary region composition intentionally mirrors the primary shape:

its own VPC

its own EKS cluster

its own ECR

its own KMS

distinct CIDR space

independent Terraform state


This is crucial for real DR.

A fake DR strategy is one where the secondary region depends too heavily on the primary region still being available.


---

G. Commands to run

1. Bootstrap remote state

cd infra/backend-bootstrap
terraform init
terraform plan \
  -var="region=us-east-1" \
  -var="state_bucket_name=examplecorp-prod-terraform-state" \
  -var="lock_table_name=examplecorp-prod-terraform-locks"
terraform apply \
  -var="region=us-east-1" \
  -var="state_bucket_name=examplecorp-prod-terraform-state" \
  -var="lock_table_name=examplecorp-prod-terraform-locks"

What these commands do

terraform init: downloads providers and initializes the working directory

terraform plan: calculates what resources Terraform will create

terraform apply: creates the S3 bucket, KMS key, and DynamoDB lock table


This is done first because later Terraform runs will depend on this backend.


---

2. Initialize the prod primary region stack

cd infra/live/prod/us-east-1
terraform init
terraform validate
terraform plan -var-file=terraform.tfvars

What these commands do

terraform init: configures the S3 backend and downloads providers

terraform validate: validates syntax and internal references

terraform plan: shows the exact infrastructure diff before creation



---

3. Apply the prod primary region

terraform apply -var-file=terraform.tfvars

What it does

Creates:

KMS keys

VPC

public/private/db subnets

IGW and NATs

ECR repos

EKS cluster and node group

RDS PostgreSQL instance



---

4. Initialize the prod secondary region stack

cd ../us-west-2
terraform init
terraform validate
terraform plan -var-file=terraform.tfvars
terraform apply -var-file=terraform.tfvars

What it does

Creates the DR region foundation.


---

5. Show outputs

terraform output

What it does

Displays useful values such as:

EKS cluster name

ECR repo URLs

RDS endpoint


These outputs are commonly fed into CI, documentation, or GitOps bootstrap steps.


---

6. Refresh state carefully

terraform plan -refresh-only -var-file=terraform.tfvars

What it does

Compares Terraform state with actual cloud resources and refreshes state without proposing config changes.

Useful for drift inspection.


---

7. Destroy protections note

For protected resources such as RDS, destruction will fail unless protections are intentionally removed. That is by design.


---

H. Production best practices

1. Separate environments by account when possible

Best enterprise pattern:

dev account

stage account

prod account

optionally separate shared-services and security accounts


This reduces blast radius and simplifies IAM boundaries.


---

2. Separate Terraform state per environment and region

Never store all environments in one state file.

A failure or accidental change in one state should not impact all environments.


---

3. Keep worker nodes private

This is a core requirement and a strong security default.

Only ingress infrastructure belongs public-facing.


---

4. Use at least three AZs where supported

For EKS and VPC design, use 3 AZs to improve spread and reduce correlated failure risk.

For RDS Multi-AZ, AWS manages the standby placement, but subnet group breadth still matters.


---

5. Use one NAT gateway per AZ in prod

A single NAT saves money but introduces avoidable failure coupling.

For prod, prefer resilience over small monthly savings.


---

6. Enable control plane logs from day one

Do not wait for an incident to realize audit logs were not enabled.


---

7. Encrypt everything with KMS

At minimum:

Terraform state

ECR

EKS secrets

RDS storage

snapshots

logs where appropriate



---

8. Enable deletion protection on stateful systems

Especially:

RDS

critical S3 buckets

DNS zones when sensitive

shared infra



---

9. Use managed services for stateful components

RDS instead of in-cluster DB is the right operational choice.


---

10. Plan CIDRs for future growth and region expansion

Do not allocate VPCs carelessly. Future peering, TGW, PrivateLink, or shared service connectivity may require non-overlapping space.


---

11. Keep Terraform modules opinionated

A mature platform does not expose 500 knobs. It exposes the right 20–40 knobs and sets secure defaults.


---

12. Run IaC security checks in CI

Use:

terraform fmt -check

terraform validate

tflint

tfsec

checkov


before merge.


---

13. Prefer SG-to-SG rules over wide CIDRs where possible

This is a more precise network authorization model than subnet-wide allowance.


---

14. Avoid hardcoding secrets in .tfvars

Use CI secret injection, Terraform Cloud variables, or secure parameter sources. The example uses a placeholder for clarity, not as a recommended secret management pattern.


---

I. Common enterprise mistakes

1. Keeping Terraform state local

This works for one engineer and fails for teams.


---

2. One monolithic Terraform state for everything

A single blast radius is an anti-pattern.

Networking, shared platform, and app-specific stacks often deserve controlled separation.


---

3. Public worker nodes

This is one of the most common bad patterns in rushed EKS setups.


---

4. Publicly accessible RDS

Major security smell. Usually unnecessary and risky.


---

5. Not enabling EKS control plane logs

Makes audit and troubleshooting much harder.


---

6. Using mutable ECR tags

Breaks release integrity and rollback confidence.


---

7. Single NAT gateway in prod without conscious acceptance

Often chosen for cost, then discovered as a hidden SPOF during an AZ issue.


---

8. No KMS customer-managed keys

Less control, less governance, weaker key lifecycle story.


---

9. No deletion protection on databases

One mistaken apply or console action can become catastrophic.


---

10. CIDR overlap across regions

This creates future pain for DR networking, replication support patterns, or cross-region service connectivity.


---

11. Flat, untagged infrastructure

Without rigorous tagging, cost ownership and audit mapping become painful.


---

12. Not designing for IRSA early

Teams often overload node roles initially and then struggle to unwind the access sprawl later.


---

13. Treating secondary region as “we will clone it later”

DR only works when the secondary region is designed as a first-class citizen from the start.


---

J. Interview explanation (Senior/Principal level)

A strong Principal-level answer for this layer would sound like this:

> I build the infrastructure layer as a modular Terraform platform with isolated state per environment and region, backed by encrypted S3 and DynamoDB locking. The VPC is segmented into public, private application, and private database subnets across multiple AZs. EKS worker nodes run only in private subnets, while ALBs sit in public subnets. The database is Amazon RDS PostgreSQL with Multi-AZ enabled, encryption at rest, backups, deletion protection, and private-only access. ECR repositories are encrypted, immutable, and scan-on-push enabled. EKS has OIDC enabled for IRSA and control plane audit/authenticator logs turned on. For DR, I model a secondary region with its own independent VPC, EKS footprint, regional ECR, and cross-region database recovery pattern so failover is architectural rather than aspirational.



Then go deeper on the architectural reasoning.

What makes this Principal-level

1. You are designing for governance, not only provisioning

A junior answer says “Terraform creates infra.” A principal answer says “Terraform is the auditable infrastructure control plane with environment isolation, policy guardrails, and blast-radius-aware state design.”


---

2. You explicitly connect network design to security posture

Private workers, public ingress, isolated DB subnets, and route separation show that the network was designed around trust boundaries.


---

3. You differentiate HA from DR

A lot of candidates confuse:

Multi-AZ RDS = regional HA

Cross-region replica/failover = DR


Being explicit about that is a strong signal.


---

4. You design around operational failure modes

Examples:

state corruption → S3 versioning + DynamoDB locking

accidental deletion → deletion protection + prevent_destroy

compromised pod credentials → IRSA instead of broad node roles

region outage → secondary region pre-provisioned

audit investigation → control plane logs enabled



---

5. You discuss trade-offs

Examples interviewers like:

NAT per AZ vs single NAT

I would say:

prod gets one NAT per AZ for resilience

non-prod can use single NAT for cost reduction


Public EKS endpoint vs private endpoint

I would say:

private-only is stronger

requires well-designed operator access

worth it for regulated or sensitive environments


RDS engine choice

I would say:

standard RDS PostgreSQL is appropriate for many enterprise apps

Aurora may be justified if failover, scaling, or global database requirements materially demand it

do not overcomplicate by default



---

K. ASCII architecture diagram

+----------------------------------+
                              |        Terraform Operators       |
                              |   CI runners / platform team     |

                                               v

                               | added above both regional stacks |
                               +-----------------------------------+


---

Layer 2 outcome

After this layer is implemented, you have:
encrypted Terraform remote state with locking
modular, reusable Terraform code
multi-AZ VPC foundation
public/private/database subnet segmentation
private EKS worker nodes
managed RDS PostgreSQL outside the cluster
immutable, scanned ECR repositories
OIDC ready for IRSA
EKS control plane audit logging
primary + secondary region infrastructure pattern for DR
audit-ready tagging and governance posture



3. Kubernetes Layer (Helm-based)

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer defines how the application runs inside Kubernetes on EKS, using Helm as the packaging standard and a GitOps-ready manifest structure as the deployment contract.

It includes:

Namespace strategy

Helm chart design

Environment-specific values

Django and React workloads

ALB-backed ingress

ServiceAccounts

IRSA-ready annotations

Requests and limits

HPA

PDB

NetworkPolicies with default deny

Hardened securityContext

Rolling update strategy

Blue/Green option

Canary option

GitOps-ready chart and release structure


This layer is where infrastructure becomes a platform runtime. Terraform gives you the cluster. Helm defines the operational contract for workloads inside it.


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

After the cloud foundation exists, the next requirement is a repeatable, controlled, policy-friendly way to deploy application workloads into Kubernetes.

Without this layer, teams usually drift into:

hand-written manifests

inconsistent resource naming

missing readiness/liveness probes

no rollout discipline

weak security contexts

environment drift

unmanaged ingress behavior

accidental privilege escalation

no clean promotion path


Helm is needed here because it gives:

consistent packaging

reusable templates

environment overlays through values files

versioned releases

strong fit for GitOps

clear chart ownership and release boundaries


At enterprise scale, Kubernetes YAML by itself is not enough. Helm gives structure, but it must be used carefully so that chart logic stays sane and environment overrides stay explicit.


---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

A Principal-level platform design uses Kubernetes as a multi-tenant orchestrator with strong workload boundaries, not just “a place to run containers.”

1. Namespaces are tenancy and blast-radius boundaries

A namespace is not a security boundary by itself, but it is still an important operational partition for:

workload grouping

RBAC scoping

quotas

policies

observability segmentation

GitOps ownership


A typical enterprise model:

platform namespaces

shared services namespaces

app namespaces by environment

optional team namespaces for internal tools



---

2. Helm charts must be opinionated

Bad Helm usage creates unreadable templating, hidden magic, and hard-to-debug releases.

A strong enterprise chart:

templates only what varies

keeps values shallow and predictable

enforces secure defaults

supports environment overrides cleanly

produces deterministic manifests



---

3. Every pod must declare resources

Without requests and limits:

scheduling becomes unpredictable

noisy neighbors emerge

autoscaling becomes misleading

cluster cost increases

reliability suffers


This is required by the constraints and is the right operational standard.


---

4. Security controls belong in workload specs, not just in security tools

Even with admission policies later, the workload definitions themselves should already be hardened:

non-root

read-only root filesystem where practical

dropped capabilities

seccomp

no privilege escalation

default deny network behavior


That reduces reliance on “catch it later” security.


---

5. PDB + HPA + rollout settings are part of availability engineering

A lot of teams think availability is handled only by infrastructure.

Not true.

Availability inside Kubernetes depends heavily on:

replica count

topology spread

readiness probes

rolling update configuration

PodDisruptionBudget

autoscaler settings

startup behavior


These belong in the chart.


---

6. Ingress needs enterprise-specific ALB annotations and operational discipline

On EKS, the AWS Load Balancer Controller with ALB is the standard fit for internet-facing traffic.

Ingress needs careful handling for:

target type

TLS

health checks

WAF integration

external DNS

idle timeout

stickiness when needed

path routing

access logs



---

7. Blue/Green and Canary should be designed up front

Even if normal rolling updates are the default, the chart and traffic model should leave room for:

Blue/Green for high-confidence cutovers

Canary for progressive exposure and risk reduction


That is what makes the deployment posture interview-worthy and production-realistic.


---

8. GitOps structure must shape the manifests now

Since CD is GitOps-driven later, the Kubernetes layer must already be arranged so that:

rendered outputs are deterministic

environment values are clearly separated

release ownership is obvious

Git diffs are reviewable

promotions are controlled



---

D. Folder structure

A realistic repository split is:

Application source repo

app-repo/
├── backend/
├── frontend/
├── helm/
│   └── fullstack-app/
│       ├── Chart.yaml
│       ├── values.yaml
│       ├── values-dev.yaml
│       ├── values-stage.yaml
│       ├── values-prod.yaml
│       ├── templates/
│       │   ├── _helpers.tpl
│       │   ├── backend-deployment.yaml
│       │   ├── backend-service.yaml
│       │   ├── backend-hpa.yaml
│       │   ├── backend-pdb.yaml
│       │   ├── backend-serviceaccount.yaml
│       │   ├── backend-networkpolicy.yaml
│       │   ├── frontend-deployment.yaml
│       │   ├── frontend-service.yaml
│       │   ├── frontend-hpa.yaml
│       │   ├── frontend-pdb.yaml
│       │   ├── frontend-networkpolicy.yaml
│       │   ├── ingress.yaml
│       │   ├── default-deny-networkpolicy.yaml
│       │   ├── configmap.yaml
│       │   ├── externalsecret.yaml
│       │   └── podmonitor-servicemonitor.yaml
│       └── README.md
└── ...

GitOps repo

gitops-repo/
├── clusters/
│   ├── dev/
│   │   ├── namespaces/
│   │   ├── platform/
│   │   └── apps/
│   │       └── fullstack-app.yaml
│   ├── stage/
│   │   └── ...
│   └── prod/
│       └── ...
├── apps/
│   └── fullstack-app/
│       ├── base/
│       │   └── values-common.yaml
│       ├── overlays/
│       │   ├── dev/
│       │   │   └── values.yaml
│       │   ├── stage/
│       │   │   └── values.yaml
│       │   └── prod/
│       │       └── values.yaml
│       └── README.md
└── argocd/
    └── ...

Why this structure works

Chart stays with the app because workload evolution is tightly coupled to app runtime needs.

GitOps repo owns what version goes where.

Environment differences remain declarative.

Promotion becomes a Git change, not an imperative command.



---

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files

1. Helm chart metadata

helm/fullstack-app/Chart.yaml

apiVersion: v2
name: fullstack-app
description: Enterprise-grade Helm chart for Django + React full-stack application
type: application
version: 0.1.0
appVersion: "2.8.4"


---

2. Base values

helm/fullstack-app/values.yaml

global:
  environment: dev
  region: us-east-1
  domain: app.example.com
  imagePullPolicy: IfNotPresent

namespace:
  create: false
  name: app-dev

backend:
  enabled: true
  replicaCount: 2

  image:
    repository: 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/fullstack-platform/backend
    tag: "2.8.4-a1b2c3d"
    digest: ""

  serviceAccount:
    create: true
    name: backend-sa
    annotations: {}

  service:
    type: ClusterIP
    port: 8000
    targetPort: 8000

  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: "250m"
      memory: "512Mi"
    limits:
      cpu: "1000m"
      memory: "1024Mi"

  autoscaling:
    enabled: true
    minReplicas: 2
    maxReplicas: 10
    targetCPUUtilizationPercentage: 70
    targetMemoryUtilizationPercentage: 75

  pdb:
    enabled: true
    minAvailable: 1

  updateStrategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxUnavailable: 0
      maxSurge: 1

  terminationGracePeriodSeconds: 60

  podSecurityContext:
    fsGroup: 10001
    seccompProfile:
      type: RuntimeDefault

  securityContext:
    runAsNonRoot: true
    runAsUser: 10001
    runAsGroup: 10001
    allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
    readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
    capabilities:
      drop:
        - ALL

  probes:
    startup:
      path: /healthz
      initialDelaySeconds: 10
      periodSeconds: 10
      timeoutSeconds: 3
      failureThreshold: 30
    liveness:
      path: /healthz
      initialDelaySeconds: 20
      periodSeconds: 15
      timeoutSeconds: 3
      failureThreshold: 3
    readiness:
      path: /readyz
      initialDelaySeconds: 10
      periodSeconds: 10
      timeoutSeconds: 3
      failureThreshold: 3

  env:
    DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE: project.settings.production
    PORT: "8000"

  secretRefs:
    app: fullstack-backend-secrets

  topologySpreadConstraints:
    enabled: true

frontend:
  enabled: true
  replicaCount: 2

  image:
    repository: 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/fullstack-platform/frontend
    tag: "2.8.4-a1b2c3d"
    digest: ""

  serviceAccount:
    create: true
    name: frontend-sa
    annotations: {}

  service:
    type: ClusterIP
    port: 8080
    targetPort: 8080

  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: "100m"
      memory: "128Mi"
    limits:
      cpu: "500m"
      memory: "256Mi"

  autoscaling:
    enabled: true
    minReplicas: 2
    maxReplicas: 6
    targetCPUUtilizationPercentage: 70
    targetMemoryUtilizationPercentage: 75

  pdb:
    enabled: true
    minAvailable: 1

  updateStrategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxUnavailable: 0
      maxSurge: 1

  terminationGracePeriodSeconds: 30

  podSecurityContext:
    fsGroup: 10001
    seccompProfile:
      type: RuntimeDefault

  securityContext:
    runAsNonRoot: true
    runAsUser: 10001
    runAsGroup: 10001
    allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
    readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
    capabilities:
      drop:
        - ALL

  probes:
    liveness:
      path: /healthz
      initialDelaySeconds: 10
      periodSeconds: 15
      timeoutSeconds: 3
      failureThreshold: 3
    readiness:
      path: /healthz
      initialDelaySeconds: 5
      periodSeconds: 10
      timeoutSeconds: 3
      failureThreshold: 3

  topologySpreadConstraints:
    enabled: true

ingress:
  enabled: true
  className: alb
  scheme: internet-facing
  certificateArn: arn:aws:acm:us-east-1:123456789012:certificate/xxxxxxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxxxxxxxxxx
  wafAclArn: arn:aws:wafv2:us-east-1:123456789012:regional/webacl/example/xxxxxxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxxxxxxxxxx
  groupName: fullstack-app
  healthcheckPath: /healthz
  sslRedirect: 443
  albAttributes:
    idle_timeout.timeout_seconds: "60"
    routing.http.drop_invalid_header_fields.enabled: "true"
    access_logs.s3.enabled: "true"
    access_logs.s3.bucket: "examplecorp-alb-logs"
    access_logs.s3.prefix: "fullstack-app"
  hosts:
    - host: app.example.com
      paths:
        - path: /
          pathType: Prefix
          serviceName: frontend
          servicePort: 8080
        - path: /api
          pathType: Prefix
          serviceName: backend
          servicePort: 8000

networkPolicy:
  enabled: true
  defaultDeny: true
  allowDns: true
  allowIngressFromAlbControllerNamespace: true

externalSecrets:
  enabled: true
  refreshInterval: 1h
  secretStoreRef:
    name: aws-secretsmanager
    kind: ClusterSecretStore
  backend:
    remoteSecretName: prod/fullstack-app/backend
    targetSecretName: fullstack-backend-secrets

serviceMonitor:
  enabled: true
  interval: 30s
  scrapeTimeout: 10s

deploymentStrategy:
  mode: rollingUpdate   # rollingUpdate | blueGreen | canary

blueGreen:
  enabled: false
  activeColor: blue

canary:
  enabled: false
  weight: 10

3. Environment overrides

helm/fullstack-app/values-dev.yaml

global:
  environment: dev
  domain: dev.app.example.com

namespace:
  name: app-dev

backend:
  replicaCount: 2
  image:
    tag: "2.8.4-a1b2c3d"
  autoscaling:
    minReplicas: 2
    maxReplicas: 4
  pdb:
    minAvailable: 1
  serviceAccount:
    annotations:
      eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/dev-fullstack-backend-irsa

frontend:
  replicaCount: 2
  image:
    tag: "2.8.4-a1b2c3d"
  autoscaling:
    minReplicas: 2
    maxReplicas: 4

ingress:
  certificateArn: arn:aws:acm:us-east-1:123456789012:certificate/dev-cert-id
  wafAclArn: arn:aws:wafv2:us-east-1:123456789012:regional/webacl/dev-waf/id
  hosts:
    - host: dev.app.example.com
      paths:
        - path: /
          pathType: Prefix
          serviceName: frontend
          servicePort: 8080
        - path: /api
          pathType: Prefix
          serviceName: backend
          servicePort: 8000

externalSecrets:
  backend:
    remoteSecretName: dev/fullstack-app/backend
    targetSecretName: fullstack-backend-secrets
