Golden signals:
- Traffic
- Errors
- Latency
- Saturation

SLO signals:
- Availability ratio
- Latency objective
- Error budget burn (fast/slow)


---

Layer 6 outcome

After this layer is implemented, you have:

kube-prometheus-stack deployed

Prometheus, Grafana, and Alertmanager in HA-aware configuration

ServiceMonitor-based application metrics collection

centralized structured logging

golden signals dashboards

SLO definitions for availability and latency

error budget burn-rate alerting

alert routing to Slack/PagerDuty

cluster and node capacity monitoring

multi-region telemetry labeling and operational visibility


7. Security & Governance Layer

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer defines the preventive, detective, and governance controls that make the platform secure by default, enforceable at scale, and reviewable during audits.

It covers:

IRSA for pod-to-AWS least privilege

AWS Secrets Manager integration through External Secrets

Admission policy enforcement with Kyverno or OPA Gatekeeper

Image security and scanning enforcement

Pod Security Standards

Network segmentation and workload isolation

Runtime security baseline

Audit logging

High-level compliance mapping for SOC 2 / CIS-aware design


This is the layer that turns a technically functional platform into an enterprise-governed platform.


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

By the time the platform reaches this layer, it can already:

build artifacts

provision infra

deploy to Kubernetes

observe workloads


But without governance and security enforcement, the system is still vulnerable to:

privilege sprawl

hardcoded secret leakage

risky pod specs

untrusted images

lateral movement

unapproved configuration drift

weak audit evidence

inconsistent security posture across teams


This layer is needed now because enterprise production does not trust “best effort” security. It requires:

guardrails

enforcement

least privilege

repeatability

evidence


In other words, this layer ensures that secure defaults are not optional developer preferences.


---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

A Principal-level security architecture is not a bag of tools. It is a layered control system.

1. Shift-left alone is insufficient

CI scanning is necessary but not enough. Something still needs to stop unsafe workloads from reaching the cluster.

That is why admission control matters.


---

2. Node IAM roles are too coarse

If all pods inherit broad node permissions indirectly, compromise of one app pod can become compromise of AWS resources.

IRSA exists specifically to break that pattern and give pods scoped permissions.


---

3. Secrets must never live in Git or images

Even private Git repos are not secret stores.

A real platform should have:

centralized secrets lifecycle

access auditability

rotation path

runtime delivery without code embedding


That is why AWS Secrets Manager + External Secrets is the right pattern here.


---

4. Governance must be automated

Security reviews do not scale if humans manually inspect every manifest.

Policy engines like Kyverno or OPA let the platform enforce:

non-root execution

no privileged pods

required resource limits

approved registries

mandatory labels

restricted hostPath usage

ingress/security constraints


That creates consistency across teams and environments.


---

5. Runtime security still matters after deployment

Even with strong CI and admission control, workloads can still behave maliciously or unexpectedly at runtime.

A baseline runtime detection layer helps identify:

suspicious process activity

shell spawns in containers

unexpected network behavior

privilege escalation attempts


This is not about promising perfect detection. It is about reducing blind spots.


---

6. Audit evidence must be easy to retrieve

For internal audit, customer assurance, incident response, and compliance reviews, the platform must provide evidence of:

who accessed what

what was deployed

what policies were enforced

whether logging existed

how secrets were handled

what IAM patterns were used


Security architecture without evidence is not enterprise-complete.


---

7. Compliance mapping should guide control intent, not become checkbox theater

SOC 2 and CIS alignment should shape controls such as:

least privilege

configuration hardening

monitoring

change management

incident response evidence


The goal is not to “pass a list.” The goal is to design controls that are actually operable.


---

D. Folder structure

A realistic security/governance layout in the GitOps repo:

gitops-repo/
├── platform/
│   ├── kyverno/
│   │   ├── values-prod.yaml
│   │   ├── policies/
│   │   │   ├── require-nonroot.yaml
│   │   │   ├── require-requests-limits.yaml
│   │   │   ├── require-approved-registries.yaml
│   │   │   ├── block-privileged.yaml
│   │   │   ├── require-networkpolicy.yaml
│   │   │   ├── require-pdb.yaml
│   │   │   ├── require-labels.yaml
│   │   │   └── verify-image-signatures.yaml
│   │   └── exceptions/
│   │       └── README.md
│   ├── external-secrets/
│   │   ├── clustersecretstore.yaml
│   │   └── iam/
│   ├── runtime-security/
│   │   ├── falco.yaml
│   │   └── rules/
│   ├── audit/
│   │   └── README.md
│   └── iam/
│       ├── irsa/
│       │   ├── backend-irsa-policy.json
│       │   ├── external-secrets-irsa-policy.json
│       │   └── fluent-bit-irsa-policy.json
│       └── README.md
├── clusters/
│   ├── prod-primary/
│   │   └── platform/
│   │       ├── kyverno.yaml
│   │       ├── external-secrets.yaml
│   │       └── runtime-security.yaml
│   └── prod-secondary/
│       └── platform/
└── apps/
    └── fullstack-app/
        ├── security/
        │   ├── networkpolicy.yaml
        │   ├── serviceaccount.yaml
        │   └── externalsecret.yaml
        └── README.md

Why this structure works:

security controls are versioned as code

policy engine rules are reviewable

exceptions have a visible home

app-specific and platform-wide controls are separated

audit evidence paths are easier to explain and maintain



---

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files

1. IRSA trust policy example

This is an AWS IAM trust relationship for a backend ServiceAccount.

platform/iam/irsa/backend-trust-policy.json

{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Principal": {
        "Federated": "arn:aws:iam::123456789012:oidc-provider/oidc.eks.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/id/EXAMPLED539D4633E53DE1B716D3041E"
      },
      "Action": "sts:AssumeRoleWithWebIdentity",
      "Condition": {
        "StringEquals": {
          "oidc.eks.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/id/EXAMPLED539D4633E53DE1B716D3041E:aud": "sts.amazonaws.com",
          "oidc.eks.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/id/EXAMPLED539D4633E53DE1B716D3041E:sub": "system:serviceaccount:app-prod:backend-sa"
        }
      }
    }
  ]
}


---

2. Backend IRSA permissions policy

Example for app access to a specific S3 bucket and decrypt permissions if needed.

platform/iam/irsa/backend-irsa-policy.json

{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Sid": "ReadSpecificAppSecrets",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "secretsmanager:GetSecretValue",
        "secretsmanager:DescribeSecret"
      ],
      "Resource": [
        "arn:aws:secretsmanager:us-east-1:123456789012:secret:prod/fullstack-app/backend-*"
      ]
    },
    {
      "Sid": "AllowKmsDecryptForAppSecrets",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "kms:Decrypt"
      ],
      "Resource": [
        "arn:aws:kms:us-east-1:123456789012:key/xxxxxxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxxxxxxxxxx"
      ]
    },
    {
      "Sid": "AppBucketAccess",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "s3:GetObject",
        "s3:PutObject",
        "s3:ListBucket"
      ],
      "Resource": [
        "arn:aws:s3:::prod-fullstack-app-artifacts",
        "arn:aws:s3:::prod-fullstack-app-artifacts/*"
      ]
    }
  ]
}

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: IRSA Trust & Permissions

12493: backend-trust-policy.json
- Federation: Establishes a secure bridge between the EKS OIDC provider and the AWS IAM service, enabling pod-level identity.

12505: "system:serviceaccount:app-prod:backend-sa"
- Strictness: Ties the IAM role *only* to a specific ServiceAccount in a specific namespace, preventing cross-tenant privilege escalation.

12521: backend-irsa-policy.json
- Least-Privilege: Explicitly grants access only to the secrets and S3 buckets required by the backend, following zero-trust principles.

12539: kms:Decrypt
- Security: Required if the backend needs to handle encrypted secrets or objects directly, ensuring only authorized pods can view sensitive data.

---

In many setups, only the External Secrets controller needs Secrets Manager read access, while the app only consumes Kubernetes Secrets. If the app does not call AWS APIs directly, remove the unnecessary permissions entirely.


---

3. ServiceAccount with IRSA annotation

helm/fullstack-app/templates/backend-serviceaccount.yaml

apiVersion: v1
kind: ServiceAccount
metadata:
  name: backend-sa
  namespace: app-prod
  annotations:
    eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/prod-fullstack-backend-irsa

This was already introduced earlier, but it belongs strongly in this layer because it is the operational binding point.


---

4. ClusterSecretStore for AWS Secrets Manager

gitops-repo/platform/external-secrets/clustersecretstore.yaml

apiVersion: external-secrets.io/v1beta1
kind: ClusterSecretStore
metadata:
  name: aws-secretsmanager
spec:
  provider:
    aws:
      service: SecretsManager
      region: us-east-1
      auth:
        jwt:
          serviceAccountRef:
            name: external-secrets-sa
            namespace: external-secrets

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: Secrets Governance

12588: kind: ClusterSecretStore
- Centralization: Defines a global, cluster-wide point of contact for AWS Secrets Manager, usable across all namespaces while maintaining central IAM control.

12597: auth: jwt
- Modernity: Uses the Kubernetes ServiceAccount token (JWT) to authenticate with AWS, eliminating the need for long-lived, high-risk IAM user credentials.

12612: kind: ExternalSecret
- Synchronization: Automates the pulling of secrets from AWS and the creation of local K8s `Secrets`, ensuring they stay in sync with the upstream source of truth.

---

This uses IRSA-backed JWT auth and avoids static AWS credentials entirely.


---

5. ExternalSecret for backend app secrets

helm/fullstack-app/templates/externalsecret.yaml

apiVersion: external-secrets.io/v1beta1
kind: ExternalSecret
metadata:
  name: backend-secrets
  namespace: app-prod
spec:
  refreshInterval: 1h
  secretStoreRef:
    name: aws-secretsmanager
    kind: ClusterSecretStore
  target:
    name: fullstack-backend-secrets
    creationPolicy: Owner
  data:
    - secretKey: DB_HOST
      remoteRef:
        key: prod/fullstack-app/backend
        property: DB_HOST
    - secretKey: DB_NAME
      remoteRef:
        key: prod/fullstack-app/backend
        property: DB_NAME
    - secretKey: DB_USER
      remoteRef:
        key: prod/fullstack-app/backend
        property: DB_USER
    - secretKey: DB_PASSWORD
      remoteRef:
        key: prod/fullstack-app/backend
        property: DB_PASSWORD
    - secretKey: DJANGO_SECRET_KEY
      remoteRef:
        key: prod/fullstack-app/backend
        property: DJANGO_SECRET_KEY

7. Kyverno policy: require non-root

gitops-repo/platform/kyverno/policies/require-nonroot.yaml

apiVersion: kyverno.io/v1
kind: ClusterPolicy
metadata:
  name: require-non-root
spec:
  validationFailureAction: Enforce
  background: true
  rules:
    - name: validate-run-as-non-root
      match:
        any:
          - resources:
              kinds:
                - Pod
      validate:
        message: "Containers must run as non-root."
        pattern:
          spec:
            securityContext:
              seccompProfile:
                type: "RuntimeDefault"
            containers:
              - securityContext:
                  runAsNonRoot: true
                  allowPrivilegeEscalation: false


---

8. Kyverno policy: require requests and limits

gitops-repo/platform/kyverno/policies/require-requests-limits.yaml

apiVersion: kyverno.io/v1
kind: ClusterPolicy
metadata:
  name: require-requests-limits
spec:
  validationFailureAction: Enforce
  background: true
  rules:
    - name: validate-resources
      match:
        any:
          - resources:
              kinds:
                - Pod
      validate:
        message: "All containers must define CPU and memory requests and limits."
        pattern:
          spec:
            containers:
              - resources:
                  requests:
                    cpu: "?*"
                    memory: "?*"
                  limits:
                    cpu: "?*"
                    memory: "?*"

9. Kyverno policy: approved registries only

gitops-repo/platform/kyverno/policies/require-approved-registries.yaml

apiVersion: kyverno.io/v1
kind: ClusterPolicy
metadata:
  name: approved-registries-only
spec:
  validationFailureAction: Enforce
  background: true
  rules:
    - name: validate-image-registry
      match:
        any:
          - resources:
              kinds:
                - Pod
      validate:
        message: "Images must come from approved ECR registries only."
        pattern:
          spec:
            containers:
              - image: "123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/* | 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-west-2.amazonaws.com/*"

Enterprise note: In practice, use Kyverno anyPattern or regex-based matching more carefully than a simplified pipe expression. The intent is approval of only regional ECR registries.

10. Kyverno policy: block privileged containers

gitops-repo/platform/kyverno/policies/block-privileged.yaml

apiVersion: kyverno.io/v1
kind: ClusterPolicy
metadata:
  name: disallow-privileged
spec:
  validationFailureAction: Enforce
  background: true
  rules:
    - name: validate-no-privileged
      match:
        any:
          - resources:
              kinds:
                - Pod
      validate:
        message: "Privileged containers are not allowed."
        pattern:
          spec:
            containers:
              - =(securityContext):
                  X(privileged): "false"

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: Kyverno Admission Control

12702: kind: ClusterPolicy
- Enforcement: Defines a global guardrail that prevents any pod from entering the cluster if it violates the specified security standards.

12706: validationFailureAction: Enforce
- Finality: In "Enforce" mode, the API server rejects the request entirely, whereas "Audit" merely logs the violation.

12724: runAsNonRoot: true
- Baseline: Blocks the most common attack vector: a container process running as UID 0 in an unhardened namespace.

12787: image: "123456789012.dkr.ecr..."
- Trust: Prevents developers from accidentally using untrusted public images from arbitrary registries, forcing the use of vetted internal repos.

---


---

11. Kyverno policy: require NetworkPolicy in namespaces

gitops-repo/platform/kyverno/policies/require-networkpolicy.yaml

apiVersion: kyverno.io/v1
kind: ClusterPolicy
metadata:
  name: require-default-deny-networkpolicy
spec:
  validationFailureAction: Audit
  background: true
  rules:
    - name: check-networkpolicy-exists
      match:
        any:
          - resources:
              kinds:
                - Namespace
      exclude:
        any:
          - resources:
              names:
                - kube-system
                - argocd
                - kyverno
                - external-secrets
      validate:
        message: "Application namespaces should contain a default deny NetworkPolicy."
        deny:
          conditions:
            any:
              - key: "{{ request.object.metadata.name }}"
                operator: Equals
                value: ""

This is illustrative; real “require related resource exists” policies are better implemented with Kyverno generate policies, namespace labels, or policy reports. The enforcement intent matters more than the simplified example.

12. Kyverno policy: require PDB

gitops-repo/platform/kyverno/policies/require-pdb.yaml

apiVersion: kyverno.io/v1

  background: true
  rules:
    - name: deployments-should-have-pdb
      match:
        any:
          - resources:
              kinds:
                - Deployment
      validate:
        message: "Critical deployments should have an associated PodDisruptionBudget."
        pattern:
          metadata:
            labels:
              app.kubernetes.io/name: "?*"

Again, enforcing cross-resource existence is more nuanced in practice. Mature teams often pair audit policies with CI/GitOps checks or Kyverno generate/verify patterns.

    pod-security.kubernetes.io/audit: restricted
    pod-security.kubernetes.io/warn: restricted
    environment: prod

This provides a strong baseline even before policy-engine-specific checks.

16. Runtime security baseline with Falco

gitops-repo/clusters/prod-primary/platform/runtime-security.yaml

apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: falco
  namespace: argocd
spec:
  project: platform
  source:
    repoURL: https://falcosecurity.github.io/charts
    chart: falco
    targetRevision: 4.15.1
    helm:
      values: |
        falco:
          jsonOutput: true
          priority: notice
          grpc:
            enabled: false
          grpcOutput:
            enabled: false

        resources:
          requests:
            cpu: 100m
            memory: 256Mi
          limits:
            cpu: 500m
            memory: 512Mi

        tty: true

        customRules:
          rules-platform.yaml: |-
            - rule: Terminal shell in container
              desc: Detect shell execution inside a running container
              condition: >
                spawned_process and container and
                proc.name in (bash, sh, zsh, ash)
              output: >
                Shell executed in container
                (user=%user.name container=%container.name pod=%k8s.pod.name ns=%k8s.ns.name cmd=%proc.cmdline)
              priority: WARNING

            - rule: Write below binary dir
              desc: Detect write activity under common binary paths
              condition: >
                open_write and container and
                fd.name startswith /usr/bin
              output: >
                Unexpected write under /usr/bin
                (container=%container.name pod=%k8s.pod.name file=%fd.name)
              priority: WARNING
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: falco
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true
    syncOptions:
      - CreateNamespace=true

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: Runtime Security (Falco)

12990: name: falco
- Visibility: Provides "Deep Packet Inspection" for the OS level, monitoring syscalls to detect suspicious behavior that static analysis cannot find.

13020: rule: Terminal shell in container
- Intrusion: Detects when a shell (bash/sh) is spawned inside a running pod—a common signature of a successful exploit or unauthorized manual hotfix.

---

Falco is one pragmatic runtime baseline. Some enterprises instead use managed CNAPP/CWPP tooling. The design principle is what matters: runtime detection exists.


---

17. Kubernetes audit logging note

EKS control plane logging was already enabled in Terraform:

api

audit

authenticator

controllerManager

scheduler


Those logs should be shipped or retained in CloudWatch with retention and access controls appropriate for audit and incident response.

18. Network segmentation example

helm/fullstack-app/templates/default-deny-networkpolicy.yaml

apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: default-deny
  namespace: app-prod
spec:
  podSelector: {}
  policyTypes:
    - Ingress
    - Egress

helm/fullstack-app/templates/backend-networkpolicy.yaml

apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: backend-allow
  namespace: app-prod
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app.kubernetes.io/component: backend
  policyTypes:
    - Ingress
    - Egress
  ingress:
    - from:
        - podSelector:
            matchLabels:
              app.kubernetes.io/component: frontend
      ports:
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 8000
  egress:
    - to:
        - ipBlock:
            cidr: 10.10.20.0/24
      ports:
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 5432
    - to:
        - namespaceSelector:
            matchLabels:
              kubernetes.io/metadata.name: kube-system
      ports:
        - protocol: UDP
          port: 53
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 53

This is part of security as much as networking.


---

19. Example Gatekeeper alternative

If using OPA Gatekeeper instead of Kyverno, a simplified constraint template pattern:

apiVersion: templates.gatekeeper.sh/v1beta1
kind: ConstraintTemplate
metadata:
  name: k8srequiredresources
spec:
  crd:
    spec:
      names:
        kind: K8sRequiredResources
  targets:
    - target: admission.k8s.gatekeeper.sh
      rego: |
        package k8srequiredresources

        violation[{"msg": msg}] {
          container := input.review.object.spec.containers[_]
          not container.resources.requests.cpu
          msg := "cpu request is required"
        }

Kyverno is often easier for Kubernetes-native policy authoring. Gatekeeper is stronger if the organization already standardizes on Rego heavily.


---

F. Line-by-line explanation of critical sections

IRSA trust policy

Principal.Federated

This is the EKS OIDC provider ARN. It tells AWS IAM that this role may be assumed by identities coming from the cluster’s OIDC provider.


---

sts:AssumeRoleWithWebIdentity

This is the IRSA-specific mechanism that lets a pod use its projected service account token to obtain AWS credentials.


---

Condition.StringEquals ... :sub

"system:serviceaccount:app-prod:backend-sa"

This is the most important scoping line.

It ensures that only the exact ServiceAccount backend-sa in namespace app-prod can assume this role.

That is least privilege at the identity boundary.


---

Backend IRSA permissions

Secrets Manager resource scoping

Permissions are scoped to a specific secret prefix, not *.

This is the difference between:

enterprise least privilege

convenience over-permissioning



---

KMS decrypt scoping

Only the CMK needed for those secrets is allowed.


---

S3 bucket scoping

Even if the app needs S3, scope it to exactly the app bucket/prefix. Do not hand out broad s3:*.


---

ClusterSecretStore

auth.jwt.serviceAccountRef

This is how External Secrets uses Kubernetes identity with IRSA to access AWS Secrets Manager.

No static AWS keys are injected. That is the correct pattern.


---

Kyverno validationFailureAction: Enforce

This means requests violating the policy are blocked at admission time.

Use Audit first for rollout when:

policy maturity is new

existing workloads need remediation

teams need visibility before hard fail


Use Enforce once the platform is ready.

This phased rollout is realistic and important.


---

background: true

This tells Kyverno to also evaluate existing resources, not only newly created ones.

That is useful for visibility and compliance posture.


---

Require non-root policy

Why both pod-level and container-level checks matter

Pod-level seccomp and container-level runAsNonRoot together enforce stronger workload hardening than a single field alone.


---

Require resources policy

This enforces a platform reliability and cost control requirement via security governance.

That is a good example of governance spanning more than just classic security.


---

Approved registries policy

This ensures only images from trusted registries, here ECR, can be deployed.

Why:

blocks shadow registries

reduces supply-chain risk

supports provenance controls


This directly supports the constraint to use ECR and not DockerHub.


---

Pod Security Standards namespace labels

enforce: restricted

This activates the restricted Pod Security Standard baseline.

It helps block risky workload patterns even before more detailed policy engine logic is considered.

A strong platform uses both:

PSS baseline

Kyverno/Gatekeeper-specific organization policies



---

Falco custom rules

spawned_process and container and proc.name in (bash, sh, zsh, ash)

This is a practical runtime signal.

For many production workloads, shell execution inside a running app container is suspicious and worth alerting on.


---

file writes under /usr/bin

This can signal tampering or unexpected behavior inside a supposedly immutable container filesystem.

Even if read-only filesystems are used, having detection is still valuable.


---

G. Commands to run

1. Verify IRSA ServiceAccount annotation

kubectl -n app-prod get sa backend-sa -o yaml

What it does

Confirms the ServiceAccount has the expected eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn annotation.


---

2. Inspect AWS identity from inside a pod

kubectl -n app-prod exec deploy/fullstack-app-fullstack-app-backend -- env | grep AWS

Or, if AWS CLI is present in a debug pod:

aws sts get-caller-identity

What it does

Verifies the pod is assuming the expected IAM role via IRSA.


---

3. Apply ClusterSecretStore

kubectl apply -f gitops-repo/platform/external-secrets/clustersecretstore.yaml

What it does

Creates the cluster-wide Secrets Manager provider definition used by External Secrets.


---

4. Check ExternalSecret sync

kubectl -n app-prod get externalsecret
kubectl -n app-prod describe externalsecret backend-secrets
kubectl -n app-prod get secret fullstack-backend-secrets

What it does

Confirms the secret reference exists, sync status is healthy, and the target Kubernetes Secret was materialized.


---

5. View Kyverno policies

kubectl get clusterpolicy

What it does

Lists the policy set currently enforcing or auditing cluster behavior.


---

6. Test a violating pod manifest

kubectl apply -f bad-pod.yaml

Where bad-pod.yaml includes:

no limits

root user

unapproved image


What it does

Confirms the admission controller blocks non-compliant workloads.

This is a key platform validation exercise.


---

7. View policy reports

kubectl get policyreport -A
kubectl get clusterpolicyreport

What it does

Shows audit/enforcement results and helps teams remediate violations.


---

8. Check Pod Security labels on namespaces

kubectl get ns app-prod --show-labels

What it does

Verifies restricted Pod Security labels are present.


---

9. View Falco alerts

kubectl -n falco logs deploy/falco

What it does

Shows runtime detection events and validates the baseline runtime security posture.


---

10. Review EKS control plane audit logs

aws logs describe-log-groups --log-group-name-prefix /aws/eks/
aws logs tail /aws/eks/fullstack-platform-prod-us-east-1-eks/control-plane --follow

What it does

Lets operators inspect control plane logs for auth, API, and audit events.


---

H. Production best practices

1. Use IRSA everywhere pods need AWS access

Do not use static AWS keys in Kubernetes Secrets.


---

2. Scope IRSA roles narrowly

One ServiceAccount, one purpose, minimal actions, minimal resource ARNs.


---

3. Keep secrets in AWS Secrets Manager, not Git

GitOps should reference secrets, not contain them.


---

4. Roll out policies in Audit first where needed

Then move to Enforce after remediation. This reduces production friction.


---

5. Enforce approved image registries

This is a simple but high-value supply-chain control.


---

6. Combine PSS with admission policies

One baseline control is not enough by itself.


---

7. Enforce resource requirements centrally

This improves security, reliability, and cost control simultaneously.


---

8. Treat policy exceptions as temporary and visible

Document:

why the exception exists

who approved it

expiry date

remediation owner



---

9. Enable runtime detection, even if basic

You do not need perfection on day one, but you do need visibility.


---

10. Retain audit logs with controlled access

They are incident-response evidence and compliance evidence.


---

11. Version policy as code

No manual drift in enforcement logic.


---

12. Periodically review IAM roles and policy reports

Least privilege erodes if not maintained.


---

I. Common enterprise mistakes

1. Using node IAM roles instead of IRSA everywhere

This leads to broad permissions and poor isolation.


---

2. Storing secrets in Helm values files

Severe anti-pattern.


---

3. Installing a policy engine but leaving everything in Audit forever

That creates visibility but not actual enforcement.


---

4. Overly broad policy exceptions

Exceptions can silently become the real policy if not controlled.


---

5. Treating Pod Security Standards as sufficient on their own

They are helpful, but not enough for enterprise-specific governance.


---

6. No runtime detection

Then teams assume prevention is perfect, which it never is.


---

7. Allowing arbitrary registries

This weakens artifact trust and provenance.


---

8. Not labeling resources consistently

Governance, cost, and compliance reporting get harder.


---

9. Poorly scoped secret access

If one workload can read all secrets, compromise blast radius is large.


---

10. No audit-log retention strategy

Evidence then disappears when it is most needed.


---

J. Interview explanation (Senior/Principal level)

A strong Principal-level explanation would sound like this:

> I design security and governance as layered controls across identity, secrets, admission, runtime, and audit. Pod access to AWS uses IRSA with tightly scoped roles, and secrets are sourced from AWS Secrets Manager through External Secrets rather than stored in Git or container images. At the admission layer I use Kyverno or Gatekeeper to enforce non-root execution, required requests and limits, approved ECR registries, and other organizational standards. Namespaces enforce restricted Pod Security Standards, network traffic is default-deny, and a runtime baseline such as Falco adds post-deployment detection for suspicious container behavior. Finally, I rely on EKS control plane audit logging and policy-as-code repositories to make the system auditable and compliance-aligned rather than security-by-convention.



What makes this Principal-level

1. You describe defense in depth

Identity, secrets, policy, runtime, and audit are all covered.

2. You connect enforcement to scale

Humans do not review everything manually; policies do.

3. You understand least privilege in practical cloud-native terms

IRSA is the key operational pattern.

4. You include evidence and auditability

That is what separates mature enterprise design from purely technical hardening.

5. You balance rollout realism

Starting some policies in Audit and maturing them into Enforce is exactly how real platforms evolve.

Good trade-off discussion

Kyverno vs OPA Gatekeeper

Kyverno is often easier for Kubernetes-native teams because policies are expressed directly in YAML-style patterns. Gatekeeper is strong when the organization already has deep Rego expertise and wants consistency with broader policy tooling.

Secrets Manager + External Secrets vs CSI Driver

Both are valid. External Secrets is often simpler when teams want native Kubernetes Secrets materialized for existing apps. CSI can reduce secret persistence in etcd for some use cases, but integration patterns differ.

Falco vs managed runtime tooling

Falco is a solid baseline. Large enterprises may later standardize on broader runtime/CNAPP tooling, but the architectural requirement is runtime visibility, not brand loyalty.


---

K. ASCII architecture diagram

             |                                         |                                       |
             v                                         v                                       v
+-------------------------+          +----------------------------+          +----------------------------+
| App Pods                |<---------| External Secrets           |          | Pod Security / Registry    |
| backend-sa / others     |          | syncs secret refs          |          | / non-root / limits / PDB  |

             |                                                                 |

             v                                                                   v
+-------------------------+                                         +----------------------------+
| Runtime Detection       |                                         | Audit Logging              |
| Falco / suspicious exec |                                         | EKS api/audit/auth logs    |
| shell / tamper signals  |                                         | CloudWatch retention       |
+-------------------------+                                         +----------------------------+

Compliance evidence sources:
- IAM role definitions
- policy-as-code repos
- audit logs
- secrets access patterns
- deployment controls
- runtime alerts


---

High-level compliance control mapping

This is not a formal audit matrix, but it is a useful control-to-architecture mapping.

SOC 2 / CIS-aware mapping

Control area	Architecture control

Least privilege access	IRSA, scoped IAM roles, ArgoCD RBAC, namespace/project boundaries
Secure secrets handling	AWS Secrets Manager, External Secrets, no secrets in Git/images
Change management	GitOps repo PRs, ArgoCD reconciliation, CI evidence, immutable images
Configuration hardening	Pod Security Standards, Kyverno/Gatekeeper policies, EKS private endpoints
Logging and monitoring	EKS control plane logs, centralized app logs, Prometheus/Alertmanager
Vulnerability management	CI Trivy scans, ECR scan-on-push, SBOM generation
Incident detection	Alerting stack, runtime detection baseline, audit logs
Availability and resilience	PDBs, HPA, Multi-AZ, DR design
Auditability	Git history, Argo history, CloudWatch logs, policy reports
Data protection	KMS encryption, private subnets, RDS encryption, controlled secret access



---

Layer 7 outcome

After this layer is implemented, you have:

IRSA-based pod IAM access

AWS Secrets Manager integrated through External Secrets

admission policy enforcement with Kyverno or OPA

approved-registry and non-root enforcement

restricted Pod Security baseline

default-deny network segmentation

runtime security detection baseline

control plane audit logging

audit-friendly policy-as-code governance

high-level SOC 2 / CIS-aligned control coverage


8. Reliability & Disaster Recovery

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer defines how the platform remains available during failures and how it recovers from partial or large-scale disasters.

It covers:

Multi-AZ resilience

Multi-region disaster recovery

Regional failover strategy

Cross-region database recovery design

Route53 health-check-based traffic failover

Backup and restore strategy

Runbooks

RTO / RPO targets

Chaos testing basics

Operational failback considerations


This layer is the difference between:

a platform that works in normal conditions

and a platform that survives real incidents


It turns “high availability” from a marketing phrase into an engineered operating capability.


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

Everything built so far gives:

hardened application artifacts

strong infrastructure

GitOps deployment

observability

policy enforcement


But none of that automatically answers the most important reliability questions:

What happens if one AZ fails?

What happens if the primary region is impaired?

What happens if the database becomes unavailable?

How fast can we restore service?

How much data can we lose?

How do operators actually execute failover?

How do we test this without waiting for a real outage?


This layer is needed because enterprise architecture must be explicit about failure handling.

A lot of systems are “resilient in theory” but fail in practice because:

runbooks are unclear

DNS failover was never validated

database promotion was never rehearsed

secrets or images were not region-ready

app assumptions about state were never tested

rollback/failback sequencing was undefined


This section closes that gap.


---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

A Principal-level reliability design clearly distinguishes between HA, resilience, and DR.

1. Multi-AZ is not multi-region DR

This is the first thing to make explicit.

Multi-AZ protects against AZ failure and many local infrastructure faults.

Multi-region DR protects against regional impairment, major control-plane issues, or broader disasters.


Teams often conflate the two. They are not the same.


---

2. RDS Multi-AZ is HA, not full DR

RDS Multi-AZ gives in-region failover and durability improvements, but if the region is impaired, it does not solve the regional failure problem.

That is why cross-region recovery design is necessary.


---

3. DR is about trade-offs, not perfection

You do not get zero RPO and instant RTO for free.

The right design depends on:

business criticality

acceptable downtime

acceptable data loss

platform budget

operational maturity


The enterprise answer is not “always active-active.” The enterprise answer is “choose the right DR posture consciously.”


---

4. The app tier is usually easier to recover than the data tier

EKS, ALB, GitOps, and stateless app pods can often be recreated or re-synced quickly.

The harder part is usually:

database state

secrets/config consistency

cache warmup and dependency recovery

DNS propagation and validation

operational confidence


So the DR architecture must center around the stateful recovery path.


---

5. A secondary region must be truly warm enough

A common anti-pattern is claiming DR exists because Terraform can create a secondary region “someday.”

Real DR requires that the secondary region already has at least:

networking

EKS cluster

platform services

regional ECR access

secrets replication strategy

DNS routing readiness

database recovery target


Otherwise the failover is rebuild, not recovery.


---

6. Runbooks matter as much as architecture

In real incidents, people do not need elegant diagrams. They need:

clear decision points

exact commands

validation steps

rollback/failback instructions

ownership and escalation paths


That is why runbooks are first-class in a mature DR design.


---

7. Reliability engineering must include testability

If failover is never tested, it is not reliable.

Chaos testing, controlled game days, and restore drills are required to validate:

assumptions

alerting

operator readiness

actual RTO/RPO behavior



---

D. Folder structure

A realistic DR and reliability structure:

gitops-repo/
├── platform/
│   ├── dr/
│   │   ├── route53/
│   │   │   ├── failover-records.yaml
│   │   │   └── health-checks.md
│   │   ├── runbooks/
│   │   │   ├── regional-failover.md
│   │   │   ├── db-promotion.md
│   │   │   ├── failback.md
│   │   │   └── backup-restore.md
│   │   ├── chaos/
│   │   │   ├── pod-failure.md
│   │   │   ├── az-evacuation.md
│   │   │   └── dns-failover-test.md
│   │   └── rto-rpo.md
│   └── backups/
│       └── policy.md
├── infra/
│   └── live/
│       ├── prod/us-east-1/
│       └── prod/us-west-2/
└── docs/
    └── incident-response/

And in the infrastructure repo:

infra/
├── modules/
│   ├── route53-failover/
│   ├── rds-postgres/
│   └── backups/
└── live/
    ├── prod/us-east-1/
    └── prod/us-west-2/

Why this matters:

DR configuration is versioned

DNS behavior is documented

runbooks live next to the platform

test procedures are reviewable and repeatable

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files

1. Route53 failover records

Terraform version, aligning with earlier infrastructure design.

infra/modules/route53-failover/main.tf

resource "aws_route53_health_check" "primary_app" {
  fqdn              = var.primary_record_fqdn
  port              = 443
  type              = "HTTPS"
  resource_path     = "/healthz"
  request_interval  = 30
  failure_threshold = 3

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.name}-primary-healthcheck"
  }
}

resource "aws_route53_record" "primary" {
  zone_id = var.zone_id
  name    = var.record_name
  type    = "A"

  set_identifier = "primary"

  failover_routing_policy {
    type = "PRIMARY"
  }

  health_check_id = aws_route53_health_check.primary_app.id

  alias {
    name                   = var.primary_alb_dns_name
    zone_id                = var.primary_alb_zone_id
    evaluate_target_health = true
  }
}

resource "aws_route53_record" "secondary" {
  zone_id = var.zone_id
  name    = var.record_name
  type    = "A"

  set_identifier = "secondary"

  failover_routing_policy {
    type = "SECONDARY"
  }

  alias {
    name                   = var.secondary_alb_dns_name
    zone_id                = var.secondary_alb_zone_id
    evaluate_target_health = true
  }
}

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: Reliability & Failover

14132: resource "aws_route53_health_check"
- Detection: Proactively monitors the `/healthz` endpoint of the primary region, providing the "heartbeat" necessary for automated failover.

14231: resource "aws_db_instance" "dr_replica"
- Data Resilience: Maintains a warm copy of the production dataset in a geographically distant region, minimizing potential data loss (RPO) during regional outages.

---

infra/modules/route53-failover/variables.tf

variable "name" {
  type = string
}

variable "zone_id" {
  type = string
}

variable "record_name" {
  type = string
}

variable "primary_record_fqdn" {
  type = string
}

variable "primary_alb_dns_name" {
  type = string
}

variable "primary_alb_zone_id" {
  type = string
}

variable "secondary_alb_dns_name" {
  type = string
}

variable "secondary_alb_zone_id" {
  type = string
}
