3. Cross-region RDS read replica

For PostgreSQL-managed RDS DR posture.

Secondary region example

resource "aws_db_instance" "dr_replica" {
  identifier             = "fullstack-platform-prod-us-west-2-postgres-dr"
  replicate_source_db    = "arn:aws:rds:us-east-1:123456789012:db:fullstack-platform-prod-us-east-1-postgres"
  instance_class         = "db.r6g.large"
  publicly_accessible    = false
  auto_minor_version_upgrade = true
  storage_encrypted      = true
  kms_key_id             = var.kms_key_arn
  copy_tags_to_snapshot  = true
  backup_retention_period = 7

  db_subnet_group_name   = aws_db_subnet_group.this.name
  vpc_security_group_ids = [aws_security_group.db.id]

  depends_on = [aws_db_subnet_group.this]
}

Enterprise note: For some workloads, standard RDS cross-region replica is sufficient. For stricter RTO/RPO or global-scale write/read demands, Aurora Global Database might be preferred, but that is a different architecture and not required here.


---

4. Backup retention and automated backups

Primary region RDS main instance excerpt

resource "aws_db_instance" "this" {
  identifier                  = var.identifier
  engine                      = "postgres"
  engine_version              = var.engine_version
  instance_class              = var.instance_class
  allocated_storage           = var.allocated_storage
  max_allocated_storage       = var.max_allocated_storage
  backup_retention_period     = 14
  backup_window               = "03:00-04:00"
  maintenance_window          = "sun:04:00-sun:05:00"
  multi_az                    = true
  storage_encrypted           = true
  copy_tags_to_snapshot       = true
  deletion_protection         = true
  enabled_cloudwatch_logs_exports = ["postgresql"]
}


---

5. Cross-region automated backup replication example

For some enterprise RDS strategies, you also want backup copy or snapshot copy in addition to or instead of a replica.

resource "aws_db_snapshot" "manual_dr_seed" {
  db_instance_identifier = aws_db_instance.this.id
  db_snapshot_identifier = "${var.identifier}-manual-dr-seed"
}

resource "aws_db_snapshot_copy" "cross_region_copy" {
  provider                       = aws.secondary
  source_db_snapshot_identifier  = aws_db_snapshot.manual_dr_seed.db_snapshot_arn
  target_db_snapshot_identifier  = "${var.identifier}-cross-region-copy"
  kms_key_id                     = var.secondary_region_kms_key_arn
  copy_tags                      = true
}

This is more often used in DR drills or lower-cost DR postures than in always-on replica designs.


---

6. Velero backup for cluster objects

Even though stateful data is in RDS, Kubernetes objects should still be recoverable.

gitops-repo/clusters/prod-primary/platform/velero.yaml

apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: velero
  namespace: argocd
spec:
  project: platform
  source:
    repoURL: https://vmware-tanzu.github.io/helm-charts
    chart: velero
    targetRevision: 7.2.1
    helm:
      values: |
        serviceAccount:
          server:
            create: true
            name: velero
            annotations:
              eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/prod-velero-irsa

        configuration:
          backupStorageLocation:
            - name: default
              provider: aws
              bucket: prod-velero-backups-us-east-1
              config:
                region: us-east-1
          volumeSnapshotLocation:
            - name: default
              provider: aws
              config:
                region: us-east-1

        schedules:
          daily-cluster-backup:
            schedule: "0 2 * * *"
            template:
              ttl: "720h"
              includedNamespaces:
                - app-prod
                - argocd
                - external-secrets
                - monitoring
                - kyverno

        resources:
          requests:
            cpu: 100m
            memory: 128Mi
          limits:
            cpu: 500m
            memory: 512Mi
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: velero
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true
    syncOptions:
      - CreateNamespace=true

This protects Kubernetes resources, not the external RDS database contents. That distinction is important.


---

7. DR readiness overlay for secondary region app

gitops-repo/apps/fullstack-app/overlays/prod-secondary/values.yaml

global:
  environment: prod
  region: us-west-2
  domain: app.example.com

namespace:
  name: app-prod

backend:
  replicaCount: 3
  image:
    repository: 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-west-2.amazonaws.com/fullstack-platform/backend
    tag: "2.8.4-a1b2c3d"
  autoscaling:
    minReplicas: 3
    maxReplicas: 12

frontend:
  replicaCount: 3
  image:
    repository: 123456789012.dkr.ecr.us-west-2.amazonaws.com/fullstack-platform/frontend
    tag: "2.8.4-a1b2c3d"
  autoscaling:
    minReplicas: 3
    maxReplicas: 8

externalSecrets:
  backend:
    remoteSecretName: prod/fullstack-app/backend-dr
    targetSecretName: fullstack-backend-secrets

ingress:
  certificateArn: arn:aws:acm:us-west-2:123456789012:certificate/prod-secondary-cert-id
  wafAclArn: arn:aws:wafv2:us-west-2:123456789012:regional/webacl/prod-secondary-waf/id
  hosts:
    - host: app.example.com
      paths:
        - path: /
          pathType: Prefix
          serviceName: frontend
          servicePort: 8080
        - path: /api
          pathType: Prefix
          serviceName: backend
          servicePort: 8000

This keeps the secondary region application stack warm and deployable.


---

8. Route53 failover runbook template

gitops-repo/platform/dr/runbooks/regional-failover.md

# Regional failover runbook

## Preconditions
- Primary region health check is failing or primary region is declared impaired
- Incident commander assigned
- Database DR target status verified
- Secondary region app health validated

## Decision criteria
1. Is this an AZ-scoped event? If yes, remain in-region if possible.
2. Is the primary region app path unavailable for > 10 minutes with no rapid remediation?
3. Is the database primary unavailable or regionally impaired?
4. Are secondary region ALB, app pods, secrets sync, and DB connectivity healthy?

## Failover steps
1. Freeze non-essential deploys.
2. Confirm GitOps sync healthy in secondary region.
3. Confirm secondary DB is promoted or writable target is ready.
4. Validate `/healthz` and `/readyz` through secondary ALB.
5. Confirm Route53 health check state for primary is failing or manually disable primary record path if needed.
6. Monitor DNS failover and external synthetic probes.
7. Confirm user traffic is reaching secondary region.
8. Notify stakeholders and record exact failover timestamp.

## Validation steps
- Successful login / core journey
- API p95 latency within acceptable degraded range
- Error rate below 2%
- DB writes succeeding
- External Secrets healthy
- Alert volume stabilized

## Exit criteria
- Secondary region serving production traffic reliably
- Incident status updated


---

9. DB promotion runbook

gitops-repo/platform/dr/runbooks/db-promotion.md

# Database promotion runbook

## Preconditions
- Decision taken to fail over to DR database
- Confirm no safe in-region recovery is available inside target RTO
- Confirm replication lag and estimated data loss

## Steps
1. Identify DR replica instance in secondary region.
2. Stop application write traffic if partial primary availability risks split-brain behavior.
3. Promote read replica to standalone writable instance.
4. Update secret or connection target for backend in secondary region if endpoint changes.
5. Force GitOps sync or restart backend pods if required to pick up new endpoint.
6. Run smoke tests:
   - schema reachable
   - read query
   - write query
   - transaction rollback test
7. Confirm app metrics and DB connection pools stabilize.

## Validation
- Writes succeed in secondary
- No app auth/session corruption
- Error rate acceptable
- Replication role no longer expected


---

10. Failback runbook

gitops-repo/platform/dr/runbooks/failback.md

# Failback runbook

## Important
Failback is not the same as failover in reverse. It must be planned and validated.

## Preconditions
- Primary region restored and stable
- Root cause understood
- Data reconciliation plan approved
- New primary target selected

## Steps
1. Keep secondary region as source of truth until reconciliation complete.
2. Rebuild or restore primary DB from current secondary data.
3. Validate application stack in primary region against restored DB.
4. Warm caches and run synthetic verification.
5. Lower DNS TTL ahead of traffic move if needed.
6. Shift traffic back in controlled manner.
7. Monitor elevated error/latency window.
8. Re-establish cross-region replica in the opposite direction.


---

11. Chaos drill document

gitops-repo/platform/dr/chaos/az-evacuation.md

# AZ evacuation drill

## Objective
Validate pod distribution, PDB behavior, node autoscaling response, and service continuity when one AZ becomes unavailable.

## Test steps
1. Cordon and drain nodes in one AZ only.
2. Observe:
   - PDB enforcement
   - Deployment health
   - HPA behavior
   - Cluster autoscaler / Karpenter replacement behavior
   - ALB target health
3. Verify:
   - No total service outage
   - Error rate stays within test objective
   - Latency degradation is temporary and bounded


---

F. Line-by-line explanation of critical sections

Route53 failover

Health check on /healthz

This allows DNS failover to be based on application-level health rather than only ALB existence.

Why that matters:

an ALB can be up while the app path is unhealthy

DNS decisions should reflect meaningful service health



---

PRIMARY / SECONDARY failover routing

This is a clean active-passive DR model.

How it behaves:

Route53 answers with primary while healthy

if health check fails, Route53 answers with secondary


This is simpler and less risky than active-active for many enterprise apps.


---

evaluate_target_health = true

Lets Route53 take the target’s AWS health into account as well.

Good defense in depth.


---

Cross-region read replica

replicate_source_db

This is the mechanism that keeps the secondary region database close enough to primary for DR purposes.

Important nuance:

this is asynchronous

therefore some replication lag may exist

therefore RPO is not zero


A principal answer should say that clearly.


---

Standby DR DB in secondary

A warm replica dramatically improves DR readiness versus restore-from-snapshot-only patterns.

Trade-off:

higher cost than snapshot-only DR

materially better RTO and usually better RPO



---

Backup retention

backup_retention_period = 14

This enables point-in-time restore capability.

Why it matters:

protects against logical corruption or accidental deletion patterns

DR is not only region failure; it is also bad data and operator mistakes



---

Backup window and maintenance window

These reduce surprise and let operations reason about expected maintenance impact.


---

Velero

Why back up Kubernetes objects if infra is in GitOps?

Because GitOps is necessary but not always sufficient for all runtime state.

Velero helps with:

cluster-scoped operational recovery

non-source-controlled objects if any exist

faster restoration of application namespace objects

disaster recovery drills


But be explicit: it does not replace database backups.


---

Secondary region overlay

This is important because the application stack in DR must already know:

which region-local ECR repo to pull from

which ACM cert to use

which WAF ACL to use

which secret source to use

what scaling baseline to maintain


That is what makes it real DR instead of theoretical DR.


---

Runbooks

Why explicit decision criteria matter

In incidents, teams often lose time debating whether to fail over.

A good runbook defines:

what thresholds matter

who decides

when to stop waiting for primary recovery


This reduces hesitation during real events.


---

G. Commands to run

1. Check Route53 failover records

aws route53 list-resource-record-sets --hosted-zone-id Z1234567890ABCDEFG

What it does

Lists the primary and secondary failover DNS records and confirms the routing policy is present.


---

2. Check Route53 health checks

aws route53 list-health-checks
aws route53 get-health-check --health-check-id <health-check-id>

What it does

Validates the health check configuration being used to drive DNS failover.


---

3. Check RDS replica status in DR region

aws rds describe-db-instances --region us-west-2 \
  --db-instance-identifier fullstack-platform-prod-us-west-2-postgres-dr

What it does

Shows the replica state, availability, endpoint, and readiness for promotion.


---

4. Promote the cross-region replica during failover

aws rds promote-read-replica \
  --region us-west-2 \
  --db-instance-identifier fullstack-platform-prod-us-west-2-postgres-dr

What it does

Converts the read replica into a standalone writable database instance in the secondary region.

This is one of the most critical DR commands.


---

5. Verify secondary region app health

curl -I https://secondary-app.example.com/healthz
curl -I https://secondary-app.example.com/readyz

What it does

Confirms the app stack in the secondary region is ready before or during failover.


---

6. Force GitOps sync in secondary region if needed

argocd app sync fullstack-app --context prod-secondary

What it does

Ensures the DR application definition is fully reconciled before traffic shifts.


---

7. List RDS snapshots

aws rds describe-db-snapshots \
  --region us-east-1 \
  --db-instance-identifier fullstack-platform-prod-us-east-1-postgres

What it does

Shows available database snapshots for restore or audit verification.


---

8. Restore from snapshot if replica path is unavailable

aws rds restore-db-instance-from-db-snapshot \
  --region us-west-2 \
  --db-instance-identifier fullstack-platform-prod-us-west-2-restore \
  --db-snapshot-identifier arn:aws:rds:us-west-2:123456789012:snapshot:restored-snapshot-id \
  --db-instance-class db.r6g.large

What it does

Creates a new DB instance from a snapshot in the DR region.

This is slower than a warm replica but is an important fallback path.


---

9. Trigger Velero backup manually

velero backup create manual-prod-backup --include-namespaces app-prod,argocd,monitoring

What it does

Creates an on-demand Kubernetes object backup.


---

10. Restore Velero backup

velero restore create --from-backup manual-prod-backup

What it does

Restores backed-up Kubernetes resources to the cluster.


---

11. Simulate pod disruption drill

kubectl drain <node-name> --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data

What it does

Evicts pods from a node to test:

PDB behavior

scheduler resilience

autoscaler behavior

app continuity


Use only in controlled drills.


---

12. Lower DNS TTL before planned failback if applicable

If using low-TTL records or planned DNS changes, validate the TTL in Route53 and any upstream caches as part of the procedure.


---

H. Production best practices

1. Define explicit RTO and RPO per service

Do not say “highly available” without numbers.


---

2. Keep the secondary region warm

At minimum:

ready cluster

platform services running

image availability

secrets strategy

DB recovery target

tested DNS path



---

3. Prefer active-passive for simpler enterprise DR unless active-active is clearly justified

Active-active adds major complexity:

write consistency

session handling

data conflict resolution

release coordination


For many full-stack apps, active-passive is the right balance.


---

4. Separate HA from DR in documentation and interviews

This shows mature systems thinking.


---

5. Test failover and restore regularly

A quarterly game day is a reasonable baseline for many enterprises.


---

6. Monitor replication lag

RPO is only meaningful if you know the current replication delay.


---

7. Keep region-local ECR images available

A failover region should not depend on primary-region registry availability.


---

8. Replicate secrets or maintain DR-region secret material

Secrets handling is a common failover blocker.


---

9. Keep DNS failover simple and observable

Do not make DNS logic too clever to operate.


---

10. Practice failback, not just failover

Failback is often more operationally dangerous than failover.


---

11. Protect backups from accidental deletion

Retention, access controls, and periodic restore validation are essential.


---

12. Ensure schema changes are DR-compatible

A backward-incompatible schema rollout can complicate recovery or failback badly.


---

I. Common enterprise mistakes

1. Calling Multi-AZ “multi-region”

This is wrong and a major interview red flag.


---

2. No defined RTO / RPO

Without targets, there is no meaningful DR design.


---

3. Only backing up the database and ignoring cluster objects

Then app/platform recovery still becomes messy.


---

4. Secondary region created but never synced or tested

This is fake DR.


---

5. No runbooks

Architecture without operator procedure is incomplete.


---

6. No replication lag monitoring

Then RPO assumptions are guesses.


---

7. DNS failover without app-level health validation

Traffic can shift to an alive but useless endpoint.


---

8. Not considering failback early

The team then improvises during recovery and increases risk.


---

9. Snapshot-only DR presented as low-RTO DR

Snapshot restore is valid, but usually slower. Be honest about the trade-off.


---

10. Unclear write handling during failover

Without decisive write freeze or promotion logic, split-brain-style operational confusion can happen.


---

J. Interview explanation (Senior/Principal level)

A strong Principal-level explanation would sound like this:

> I separate in-region high availability from cross-region disaster recovery. Within the primary region, the platform is Multi-AZ end to end: ALB across AZs, EKS worker nodes spread across private subnets in multiple AZs, and RDS Multi-AZ for database HA. For regional disaster recovery, I maintain a warm secondary region with pre-provisioned networking, EKS, platform services, and application definitions, plus region-local ECR images and secrets integration. Route53 uses health-check-based failover from the primary ALB to the secondary ALB, and the database recovery path is based on a cross-region RDS replica or snapshot restore strategy depending on the chosen RTO/RPO trade-off. I document exact failover, promotion, and failback runbooks and validate them through controlled game days and chaos drills.



What makes this Principal-level

1. You distinguish failure domains clearly

AZ, region, cluster, and database failure paths are not mixed together.

2. You tie DR architecture to business objectives

RTO and RPO are explicit design inputs, not afterthoughts.

3. You include operational execution, not just topology

Runbooks and drills are first-class.

4. You are honest about trade-offs

Cross-region replica costs more but gives better RTO than snapshot-only recovery.

5. You think about failback

That is a common gap in weaker designs.

Good trade-off discussion

Cross-region replica vs snapshot restore

Replica: higher steady-state cost, faster recovery, better RPO

Snapshot restore: lower cost, slower recovery, usually worse RPO/RTO


Active-passive vs active-active

Active-passive is often the right production choice for a Django/React enterprise app because it is simpler, more auditable, and lower-risk operationally.

Route53 failover vs GSLB-style advanced traffic steering

Route53 failover is usually sufficient and easier to reason about. More advanced global traffic approaches are only justified if the business really needs them.


---

K. ASCII architecture diagram

+--------------------------------------+
                               |            Route53 DNS               |
                               |   PRIMARY / SECONDARY failover       |
                               |   health check on /healthz           |
                               +------------------+-------------------+
                                                  |
                        +-------------------------+-------------------------+
                        |                                                   |
                        v                                                   v
         +----------------------------------+               +----------------------------------+
         |   PRIMARY REGION (us-east-1)     |               | SECONDARY REGION (us-west-2)    |
         |                                  |               |                                  |
         |  ALB -> EKS -> App Pods          |               |  ALB -> EKS -> App Pods          |
         |  Multi-AZ private worker nodes   |               |  Warm standby / ready platform   |
         |  RDS PostgreSQL Multi-AZ         |               |  DR overlay synced via GitOps    |
         |  Source of truth during normal   |               |  Region-local ECR + secrets      |
         +----------------+-----------------+               +----------------+-----------------+
                          |                                                  ^
                          |                                                  |
                          v                                                  |
         +----------------------------------+               +----------------+-----------------+
         | RDS cross-region replication     |-------------->|  DR read replica / restore      |
         | async replication to secondary   |               |  promote to writable on failover |
         +----------------------------------+               +----------------------------------+

Additional recovery controls:
- Velero backs up Kubernetes objects
- RDS automated backups and snapshots retained
- Runbooks guide failover, DB promotion, and failback
- Chaos drills validate AZ loss and recovery assumptions


---

RTO / RPO explanation

A realistic example target set for this architecture:

Primary production service targets

AZ-level failure

RTO: near-zero to a few minutes

RPO: zero or near-zero from app perspective

Why:

ALB and EKS are Multi-AZ

RDS Multi-AZ handles in-region DB failover



Regional failover with cross-region read replica

RTO: 15–30 minutes typical target

RPO: seconds to a few minutes depending on replication lag

Why:

secondary app stack already exists

DB promotion still takes coordinated action

DNS failover and app validation still required



Regional failover with snapshot restore only

RTO: 1–4+ hours depending on DB size and automation maturity

RPO: last successful snapshot / backup point

Why:

restore is slower

more operational steps

more validation required



These numbers are example enterprise targets, not guarantees. They must be validated through drills.


---

Backup strategy

Database

RDS automated backups enabled

point-in-time recovery retained for 14 days

snapshots copied with tags

optionally copy snapshots cross-region

monitor backup success/failure


Kubernetes objects

Velero scheduled backups for critical namespaces

include app, GitOps, monitoring, policy, secrets-controller namespaces

test restore regularly


Terraform / infra metadata

S3 versioned remote state

DynamoDB lock table

Git repos as declarative source of truth


Container artifacts

ECR immutable images

region replication

lifecycle retention sized for rollback horizon



---

Restore procedures

DB logical or operational restore

1. Identify corruption or recovery point


2. Select PITR target or snapshot


3. Restore into isolated instance


4. Validate schema/app compatibility


5. Cut app over intentionally



Cluster object restore

1. Confirm cluster exists and platform baseline is healthy


2. Restore critical namespaces via Velero if needed


3. Reconcile GitOps apps


4. Validate secrets, ingress, and monitoring



Full regional recovery

1. Promote or restore DR DB


2. Verify secondary platform services


3. Reconcile GitOps app in secondary


4. Validate synthetic checks


5. Allow DNS failover or trigger planned cutover


6. Monitor stabilization




---

Chaos testing basics

A mature baseline chaos program for this architecture should include:

1. Pod termination drill

Kill backend/frontend pods and verify:

rollout/controller recovery

no user-visible outage beyond tolerance

alerts behave as expected


2. Node drain drill

Drain one node and verify:

PDB enforcement

autoscaler/Karpenter reaction

no major latency spike


3. Single-AZ disruption drill

Remove one AZ worth of nodes and verify:

topology spread worked

capacity headroom sufficient

service stayed available


4. Database failover rehearsal

Exercise planned RDS failover or DR promotion in non-prod.

5. DNS failover drill

Validate:

Route53 behavior

synthetic probes

stakeholder communication

dashboard visibility


6. Restore drill

Restore from snapshot or backup into isolated environment and verify app correctness.

The point is not chaos for theater. The point is confidence in assumptions.


---

Layer 8 outcome

After this layer is implemented, you have:

Multi-AZ resilience across app and data tiers

a real multi-region DR pattern

Route53 health-check-based failover

cross-region RDS recovery design

backup and restore strategy for DB and Kubernetes objects

explicit failover, promotion, and failback runbooks

defined RTO/RPO targets

a practical chaos and game-day validation model


9. Cost Optimization Strategy

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer defines how to operate the platform with production-grade cost discipline without weakening reliability, security, or deployment safety.

It covers:

Instance sizing strategy

Node group mix and purchasing model

Spot usage for non-prod

Autoscaling guardrails

Right-sizing workloads

Storage and log retention tuning

Cost allocation and chargeback tags

Cost visibility and alerting

Regional DR cost trade-offs

Continuous optimization process


At Principal level, cost optimization is not “make it cheaper.” It is resource economics aligned to service objectives.


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

By now, the architecture is:

multi-environment

multi-AZ

multi-region recoverable

observable

policy enforced

GitOps managed


All of those improve enterprise readiness, but they also create real cost surfaces:

always-on EKS clusters

NAT gateways

ALBs

Prometheus storage

CloudWatch / logging volume

RDS Multi-AZ

secondary-region readiness

overprovisioned requests and limits

waste from long artifact retention

idle non-prod environments


Without deliberate cost controls, even a well-built platform becomes inefficient.

This layer is needed because mature platform engineering must answer:

what costs are intentional?

what costs are waste?

where do we trade money for resilience?

what should be different in dev, stage, and prod?

how do we prove we are using resources responsibly?



---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

A Principal-level cost strategy treats cost as a design dimension, not an afterthought.

1. Reliability and cost must be balanced, not opposed

Some costs are justified because they buy resilience:

NAT per AZ in prod

RDS Multi-AZ

warm DR region

HA observability stack


The goal is not to eliminate these. The goal is to optimize around them intelligently.


---

2. Most Kubernetes waste comes from bad sizing

In real enterprises, major Kubernetes waste often comes from:

inflated requests

no limits discipline

too many replicas

always-on low-utilization workloads

oversized node types

too much idle headroom


That means the best cost strategy is often:

better workload sizing

better autoscaling

better retention tuning


not random infrastructure cuts.


---

3. Environments should not cost the same

Prod, stage, and dev should have different cost postures.

Typical pattern:

prod: reliability-first, optimized but not fragile

stage: functionally representative, lower steady-state scale

dev: cheap, scalable down aggressively, often spot-friendly


A platform that treats all environments equally is wasting money.


---

4. Multi-region DR is a conscious spend

A warm secondary region is valuable, but not free.

The organization must deliberately choose between:

warm standby

pilot-light

backup/restore only


This architecture uses a warm standby pattern. That is appropriate for stronger RTO/RPO, but it must be communicated as an intentional cost decision.


---

5. Cost attribution matters as much as optimization

If the organization cannot answer “who owns this spend?” optimization slows down.

That is why:

tags

labels

cost categories

namespace/team mapping


are crucial.


---

6. Autoscaling can both save and waste money

Autoscaling is not automatically cost-efficient.

Poor autoscaling can cause:

too many tiny nodes

churn

overreaction to spikes

HPA scaling on noisy signals

expensive scale-outs caused by inflated requests


So cost-aware autoscaling needs guardrails.


---

7. Observability itself needs cost controls

Logging and metrics systems can quietly become major spend drivers through:

excessive retention

high cardinality

verbose logs

unnecessary scrape frequency

over-retained dashboards/artifacts


This must be governed, not left organic.


---

D. Folder structure

A realistic cost-governance structure:

gitops-repo/
├── platform/
│   ├── cost/
│   │   ├── dashboards/
│   │   │   ├── cluster-cost.json
│   │   │   ├── namespace-cost.json
│   │   │   └── infra-cost-summary.json
│   │   ├── alerts/
│   │   │   ├── budget-alerts.yaml
│   │   │   └── idle-capacity-alerts.yaml
│   │   ├── policies/
│   │   │   ├── required-cost-labels.yaml
│   │   │   └── right-sizing-guidelines.md
│   │   └── README.md
│   ├── kube-prometheus-stack/
│   └── logging/
├── apps/
│   └── fullstack-app/
│       ├── overlays/
│       │   ├── dev/values.yaml
│       │   ├── stage/values.yaml
│       │   └── prod-primary/values.yaml
│       └── resources/
│           └── sizing-notes.md
└── docs/
    └── finops/
        ├── environment-policy.md
        ├── retention-policy.md
        └── dr-cost-model.md

And in Terraform:

infra/
├── modules/
│   ├── eks/
│   ├── rds-postgres/
│   ├── tags/
│   └── cost-controls/
└── live/
    ├── dev/
    ├── stage/
    └── prod/

This matters because cost strategy should be codified and reviewable, not tribal knowledge.


---

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files

1. Environment-specific node strategy in Terraform

Prod primary EKS node group baseline

module "eks" {
  source                    = "../../../modules/eks"
  cluster_name              = "${local.name}-eks"
  cluster_version           = "1.31"
  vpc_id                    = module.vpc.vpc_id
  private_subnet_ids        = module.vpc.private_app_subnet_ids
  endpoint_public_access    = false
  endpoint_private_access   = true
  eks_kms_key_arn           = module.kms_eks.key_arn
  enabled_cluster_log_types = ["api", "audit", "authenticator", "controllerManager", "scheduler"]

  node_instance_types       = ["m7i.large"]
  node_desired_size         = 3
  node_min_size             = 3
  node_max_size             = 10
}

This is the on-demand production baseline.


---

Dev cluster cheaper baseline

module "eks" {
  source                    = "../../../modules/eks"
  cluster_name              = "${local.name}-eks"
  cluster_version           = "1.31"
  vpc_id                    = module.vpc.vpc_id
  private_subnet_ids        = module.vpc.private_app_subnet_ids
  endpoint_public_access    = false
  endpoint_private_access   = true
  eks_kms_key_arn           = module.kms_eks.key_arn
  enabled_cluster_log_types = ["api", "audit", "authenticator"]

  node_instance_types       = ["m7i.large"]
  node_desired_size         = 2
  node_min_size             = 1
  node_max_size             = 4
}

Dev keeps strong security posture but scales smaller.


---

2. Spot node group for non-prod

Example additional managed node group for dev/stage only.

resource "aws_eks_node_group" "spot" {
  cluster_name    = aws_eks_cluster.this.name
  node_group_name = "${var.cluster_name}-spot"
  node_role_arn   = aws_iam_role.node.arn
  subnet_ids      = var.private_subnet_ids

  ami_type       = "AL2023_x86_64_STANDARD"
  capacity_type  = "SPOT"
  instance_types = ["m7i.large", "m6i.large", "m5.large"]

  scaling_config {
    desired_size = 1
    min_size     = 0
    max_size     = 8
  }

  update_config {
    max_unavailable_percentage = 50
  }

  labels = {
    workload = "spot"
    lifecycle = "ec2spot"
  }

  taint {
    key    = "spotOnly"
    value  = "true"
    effect = "NO_SCHEDULE"
  }

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.cluster_name}-spot-ng"
    "k8s.io/cluster-autoscaler/enabled"             = "true"
    "k8s.io/cluster-autoscaler/${var.cluster_name}" = "owned"
  }
}

This lets non-critical workloads land on cheaper compute.


---

3. Karpenter NodePool for cost-aware burst capacity

If using Karpenter instead of or alongside Cluster Autoscaler, a realistic cost-aware configuration:

apiVersion: karpenter.sh/v1
kind: NodePool
metadata:
  name: general-nonprod
spec:
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        workload: general
        environment: dev
    spec:
      requirements:
        - key: kubernetes.io/arch
          operator: In
          values: ["amd64"]
        - key: karpenter.sh/capacity-type
          operator: In
          values: ["spot", "on-demand"]
        - key: node.kubernetes.io/instance-type
          operator: In
          values: ["m7i.large", "m6i.large", "m5.large", "c6i.large"]
      taints:
        - key: spotOnly
          value: "true"
          effect: NoSchedule
  disruption:
    consolidationPolicy: WhenEmptyOrUnderutilized
    consolidateAfter: 5m
  limits:
    cpu: "100"
    memory: 200Gi

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: Cost Optimization

15745: capacity_type = "SPOT"
- Economy: Dramatically reduces compute spend for non-production environments by leveraging spare AWS capacity (often at a 60-90% discount).

15811: consolidationPolicy: WhenEmptyOrUnderutilized
- Efficiency: Instructs Karpenter to actively move pods and de-provision nodes that are underutilized, ensuring the cluster size perfectly matches the actual workload demand.

15994: resource "aws_budgets_budget"
- Governance: Establishes a hard financial boundary for the production platform, triggering alerts when spend exceeds 80% of the allocated monthly forecast.

---

This helps prevent permanent overprovisioning and reduces idle-cost waste in non-prod.


---

4. Resource sizing in Helm for backend and frontend

Prod-primary values excerpt

backend:
  replicaCount: 4
  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: "250m"
      memory: "512Mi"
    limits:
      cpu: "1000m"
      memory: "1024Mi"
  autoscaling:
    enabled: true
    minReplicas: 4
    maxReplicas: 20
    targetCPUUtilizationPercentage: 70
    targetMemoryUtilizationPercentage: 75

frontend:
  replicaCount: 4
  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: "100m"
      memory: "128Mi"
    limits:
      cpu: "500m"
      memory: "256Mi"
  autoscaling:
    enabled: true
    minReplicas: 4
    maxReplicas: 12
    targetCPUUtilizationPercentage: 70
    targetMemoryUtilizationPercentage: 75

Dev values excerpt

backend:
  replicaCount: 2
  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: "150m"
      memory: "384Mi"
    limits:
      cpu: "500m"
      memory: "768Mi"
  autoscaling:
    enabled: true
    minReplicas: 2
    maxReplicas: 4

frontend:
  replicaCount: 2
  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: "75m"
      memory: "96Mi"
    limits:
      cpu: "250m"
      memory: "192Mi"
  autoscaling:
    enabled: true
    minReplicas: 2
    maxReplicas: 4

This demonstrates environment-aware right-sizing.


---

5. Log retention tuning

CloudWatch Log Group retention example

resource "aws_cloudwatch_log_group" "eks_control_plane" {
  name              = "/aws/eks/${var.cluster_name}/control-plane"
  retention_in_days = 30
  kms_key_id        = var.logs_kms_key_arn
}

Application log group example

resource "aws_cloudwatch_log_group" "app_logs" {
  name              = "/eks/prod-primary/application"
  retention_in_days = 14
  kms_key_id        = var.logs_kms_key_arn
}

Suggested pattern:

control plane audit logs: longer retention

app debug logs: shorter hot retention

archive if compliance requires longer history



---

6. Prometheus retention tuning

values-prod-primary.yaml excerpt

prometheus:
  prometheusSpec:
    retention: 15d
    retentionSize: "150GB"
    scrapeInterval: 30s
    evaluationInterval: 30s

Non-prod example

prometheus:
  prometheusSpec:
    retention: 5d
    retentionSize: "40GB"
    scrapeInterval: 60s
    evaluationInterval: 60s

This reduces observability spend where ultra-granular long retention is not justified.


---

7. ECR lifecycle policy

infra/modules/ecr/main.tf excerpt

resource "aws_ecr_lifecycle_policy" "this" {
  repository = aws_ecr_repository.this.name

  policy = jsonencode({
    rules = [
      {
        rulePriority = 1
        description  = "Expire untagged images after 7 days"
        selection = {
          tagStatus   = "untagged"
          countType   = "sinceImagePushed"
          countUnit   = "days"
          countNumber = 7
        }
        action = {
          type = "expire"
        }
      },
      {
        rulePriority = 2
        description  = "Retain last 200 tagged images"
        selection = {
          tagStatus     = "tagged"
          tagPrefixList = ["build-", "v"]
          countType     = "imageCountMoreThan"
          countNumber   = 200
        }
        action = {
          type = "expire"
        }
      }
    ]
  })
}

This prevents registry sprawl while keeping rollback history.


---

8. AWS Budgets alert example

resource "aws_budgets_budget" "prod_monthly" {
  name         = "prod-platform-monthly-budget"
  budget_type  = "COST"
  limit_amount = "15000"
  limit_unit   = "USD"
  time_unit    = "MONTHLY"

  cost_filters = {
    TagKeyValue = [
      "Environment$prod"
    ]
  }

  notification {
    comparison_operator        = "GREATER_THAN"
    threshold                  = 80
    threshold_type             = "PERCENTAGE"
    notification_type          = "ACTUAL"
    subscriber_email_addresses = ["platform-finops@example.com", "sre@example.com"]
  }

  notification {
    comparison_operator        = "GREATER_THAN"
    threshold                  = 100
    threshold_type             = "PERCENTAGE"
    notification_type          = "FORECASTED"
    subscriber_email_addresses = ["platform-finops@example.com", "engineering-director@example.com"]
  }
}

This provides basic spend alerting.

9. Standard cost/allocation tags

Shared local tags

locals {
  common_tags = {
    Application        = var.application_name
    Environment        = var.environment
    ManagedBy          = "Terraform"
    Owner              = var.owner
    CostCenter         = var.cost_center
    ComplianceScope    = var.compliance_scope
    DataClassification = var.data_classification
    Region             = var.aws_region
    Platform           = "fullstack-platform"
  }
}

These tags enable cost breakdown and accountability.
