---

5. Change management policy

governance/change-policy.md

# Change policy

## Standard changes
Examples:
- application version promotion through GitOps
- routine Helm values updates
- non-breaking autoscaling adjustments
- dashboard/rule updates with rollback path

Approval:
- code review in owning repo
- CI checks green
- GitOps sync validation

## Elevated-risk changes
Examples:
- database engine upgrades
- ingress controller major version changes
- cluster version upgrades
- network policy tightening for tier-1 apps
- production policy engine enforcement flips Audit -> Enforce

Approval:
- owning team approval
- platform approval
- SRE review
- change window if prod-impacting

## Emergency changes
Examples:
- security containment
- incident rollback
- urgent config revert

Requirements:
- incident or change ticket reference
- follow-up PR to restore Git truth if break-glass used
- post-change review within 1 business day


---

6. Exception policy

governance/exception-policy.md

# Exception policy

All exceptions to platform standards must include:

- control being bypassed
- business reason
- risk assessment
- compensating controls
- owner
- expiry date
- review date

## Examples
- temporary privileged initContainer
- temporary policy exemption for a legacy workload
- temporary higher log retention for an investigation

## Rules
- no permanent exceptions without architecture review
- all prod exceptions require security + platform sign-off
- expired exceptions are treated as non-compliant


---

7. Golden path web service template

service-catalog/golden-path-web-service.md

# Golden path: Web service

Required:
- Helm chart derived from platform template
- requests and limits on all containers
- readiness, liveness, startup probes
- default deny NetworkPolicy
- PDB
- ServiceMonitor
- ExternalSecret or approved secret source
- IRSA if AWS access required
- owner.yaml, support.yaml, slo.yaml committed
- rollback procedure documented

Optional:
- canary rollout
- blue/green rollout
- Redis/cache sidecar-free external integration


---

8. Platform SLA / OLA definition

support/support-model.md

# Support model

## Platform engineering SLA
- P1 platform incident acknowledgment: 15 minutes
- P2 platform incident acknowledgment: 30 minutes
- Standard platform request response: 2 business days
- Golden path onboarding support: 5 business days

## Shared operational responsibilities (OLAs)
Platform engineering owns:
- EKS platform
- ArgoCD
- ingress controllers
- External Secrets
- policy engine
- observability platform

Application teams own:
- service code
- service dashboards
- service SLO adherence
- application rollback decision
- app-specific incident remediation

SRE owns:
- incident coordination for major events
- reliability policy
- SLO governance
- DR drill facilitation

10. Break-glass access runbook

runbooks/break-glass-access.md

# Break-glass access

## Purpose
Emergency-only elevated access for production incident containment.

## Rules
- ticket or incident ID required
- minimum necessary access
- time-bound access only
- all actions logged
- GitOps truth restored after emergency
- post-use review required within 1 business day

## Typical use cases
- ArgoCD unavailable during incident
- urgent cluster diagnosis
- emergency secret rotation
- containment action for active security event


---

11. Team topology model

governance/ownership-matrix.md

# Team topology

## Platform Engineering
Owns:
- Kubernetes platform
- GitOps controllers
- CI shared libraries
- ingress and networking platform components
- secrets platform integration
- baseline observability stack

## SRE / Production Engineering
Owns:
- incident management framework
- reliability standards
- SLO and error budget governance
- DR exercises
- availability reviews

## Security Engineering
Owns:
- policy requirements
- exception review
- IAM governance standards
- control mapping
- audit support

## Application Teams
Own:
- business services
- domain-specific alerts and dashboards
- code-level rollback
- application runbooks
- feature delivery and service reliability

12. Platform maturity model

platform-roadmap/maturity-model.md

# Platform maturity model

## Level 1
- Basic CI/CD
- Shared cluster
- minimal standards

## Level 2
- GitOps
- standard Helm charts
- centralized logging and metrics
- baseline security policies

## Level 3
- strong paved roads
- SLO-driven operations
- DR runbooks tested
- cost allocation and right-sizing

## Level 4
- multi-region tested failover
- policy exception lifecycle managed
- ML platform integrated
- cross-cloud migration playbooks validated

## Level 5
- platform product model
- self-service onboarding
- automated evidence collection
- reliability/cost/security objectives actively optimized


---

F. Line-by-line explanation of critical sections

Ownership metadata

This is one of the most underrated practices in enterprise platforms.

A service should never exist in production without clear answers to:

who owns it?

who is paged?

who approves risk decisions?

who depends on it?


Encoding that as versioned metadata improves:

incident handling

audit readiness

onboarding

support routing



---

SLO policy with release freeze

freeze_release_on_breach: true

This is how error budgets become an operating mechanism, not just a dashboard.

Interpretation:

if the service is out of budget, release velocity is intentionally slowed

exceptions require explicit business and reliability sign-off


That is mature SRE governance.


---

Change policy

The distinction between:

standard changes

elevated-risk changes

emergency changes


is critical.

Without this, every change is either over-bureaucratic or under-controlled.

The best enterprise models keep low-risk work fast and high-risk work deliberate.


---

Exception policy with expiry

This is one of the strongest governance controls you can add.

The key rule is: exceptions expire

Otherwise every temporary bypass becomes the permanent operating model.


---

Golden path

A golden path is not just documentation. It is the default platform contract.

It reduces:

onboarding time

design drift

security gaps

platform support burden


A great platform team makes the safest path the easiest path.


---

Platform SLA vs service SLO

This distinction matters.

Platform SLA/OLA

What the platform team commits to for internal capabilities.

Service SLO

What the application team commits to for end-user reliability.

Mixing them causes confusion.


---

Break-glass access

A mature platform accepts that emergencies happen.

The goal is not “nobody can ever do anything manually.” The goal is:

manual emergency access is controlled

logged

temporary

reviewed

reconciled back into Git truth afterward


That is much better than pretending no emergency path exists.


---

Team topology

This section matters because platform failure often comes from ownership overlap.

For example:

platform thinks app team owns alerts

app team thinks platform owns alerts

nobody owns them


Principal-level design removes that ambiguity.


---

G. Commands to run

1. Validate service metadata exists before prod promotion

In CI, a simple check can enforce ownership files:

test -f gitops-repo/apps/fullstack-app/owner.yaml
test -f gitops-repo/apps/fullstack-app/slo.yaml
test -f gitops-repo/apps/fullstack-app/support.yaml

What it does

Fails promotion if required operational metadata is missing.


---

2. Render readiness checklist into PR review flow

grep -q "IRSA configured:" governance/production-readiness-review.md

What it does

Simple example that ensures the PRR template exists and can be part of release readiness automation.


---

3. Check on-call ownership during incident triage

Operationally, responders should be able to read:

cat gitops-repo/apps/fullstack-app/support.yaml

What it does

Provides immediate ownership and paging context during response.


---

4. Run quarterly ownership audit

find gitops-repo/apps -name owner.yaml -print

What it does

Enumerates service ownership files for review and recertification.


---

5. Validate every service has a documented SLO

find gitops-repo/apps -name slo.yaml -print

What it does

Supports platform governance reporting and PRR enforcement.


---

H. Production best practices

1. Treat the platform as a product

Have:

roadmap

customers

service levels

onboarding experience

deprecation policy

docs


Not just clusters and tickets.


---

2. Make ownership machine-readable

Store owners, support paths, and SLO metadata in Git.


---

3. Keep app teams accountable for their service

Platform should enable, not absorb, all service ownership.


---

4. Create paved roads for common workload types

Web service, worker, cron job, ML service.


---

5. Keep emergency processes explicit

Break-glass paths should exist and be reviewable.


---

6. Use postmortems to improve the platform, not just services

Many incidents reveal platform gaps, not only app bugs.


---

7. Limit ticket-based operational dependency

Push toward self-service with guardrails.


---

8. Review exceptions regularly

Expired exceptions should not survive by inertia.


---

9. Tie reliability policy to release policy

Error budgets should influence deployment pace.


---

10. Perform regular readiness reviews for tier-1 services

Do not let critical services drift below production standards over time.


---

I. Common enterprise mistakes

1. Platform team owns everything in practice

This creates bottlenecks and weak service accountability.


---

2. App teams have full freedom with no paved road

This leads to fragmentation and security drift.


---

3. No explicit ownership metadata

Incidents then waste time figuring out who is responsible.


---

4. Exceptions without expiry

This is how control posture silently erodes.


---

5. No formal incident severity model

Then communication and escalation become inconsistent.


---

6. No production readiness review

Critical services launch missing runbooks, alerts, or rollback plans.


---

7. Platform docs live only in people’s heads

This breaks onboarding and audit readiness.


---

8. Error budgets are tracked but ignored

Then SRE policy has no real effect.


---

J. Interview explanation (Senior/Principal level)

A strong Principal-level explanation would sound like this:

> I treat platform engineering as an operating model, not just a tool stack. The platform team owns the paved road and the shared runtime capabilities such as Kubernetes, GitOps, observability, policy, and secrets integration, while application teams remain accountable for their services, alerts, and on-call ownership. I encode ownership, SLOs, and escalation metadata in Git so support routing and governance are explicit. I also define change classes, production-readiness reviews, exception expiry rules, and break-glass procedures so the platform remains operable under both normal and emergency conditions. That gives the organization a scalable model for day-2 operations instead of relying on tribal knowledge or heroics.



What makes this Principal-level

1. It connects architecture to organization

Strong systems fail if ownership is weak. This layer solves that.

2. It treats platform as a product

Roadmap, golden paths, internal customers, and SLAs are all present.

3. It scales governance without paralyzing delivery

Guardrails, self-service, and risk-based change control are balanced.

4. It recognizes day-2 operations as a first-class design concern

This is a strong differentiator in staff-level interviews.

Layer 12 outcome

After this layer is added, the architecture now also includes:

a clear team topology

production readiness review process

incident severity and escalation model

platform maturity model

a true day-2 operating model suitable for enterprise scale


This brings the full design from pure technical architecture into platform product + enterprise operating model territory.

If you want, I can also add Layer 13: Data Platform & Analytics Layer so the architecture covers batch/stream ingestion, lakehouse/warehouse, CDC, ETL/ELT, and governance around analytics data.



13. Data Platform & Analytics Layer

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer defines the enterprise data platform that supports:

operational analytics

BI reporting

product analytics

batch pipelines

streaming pipelines

CDC

warehouse/lakehouse workloads

governed datasets

self-service analytics

data quality and lineage


It is the layer that turns application data into a reliable, governed, scalable analytical asset.

For this full-stack architecture, it covers:

OLTP to analytics data movement

event collection from Django/React

CDC from PostgreSQL

S3-based lake or lakehouse zones

warehouse/lake query engines

orchestration

transformations

data contracts

quality validation

metadata, catalog, and access controls


This is not just “set up a data warehouse.” It is the operating system for trustworthy analytics and downstream data products.


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

The platform already supports:

application delivery

infra automation

Kubernetes runtime

observability

security

DR

MLOps


But enterprises also need to answer questions like:

how many users signed up today?

what is conversion by cohort?

which feature release changed retention?

what is the daily revenue by segment?

can finance trust this dashboard?

can data science access curated training data safely?

how do we feed downstream ML features and BI without overloading production RDS?


Without a proper data platform, teams usually drift into:

direct reporting queries on production RDS

duplicated CSV exports

inconsistent definitions

shadow ETL scripts

no lineage

weak access controls

data quality surprises

dashboard trust erosion


This layer is needed because enterprise platforms must support both transaction processing and decision support cleanly.


---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

A Principal-level data platform design starts by separating workloads.

1. OLTP and analytics should not share the same serving path

The Django app and production PostgreSQL are optimized for:

transactions

user-facing reads/writes

consistency for application logic


They are not optimized for:

large scans

heavy aggregations

BI concurrency

ad hoc analytics queries


So the architecture must move data out of the operational path into analytics systems.


---

2. Use multiple ingestion patterns, not one

Different data types need different movement patterns:

CDC for database tables

application events for product analytics

batch exports for periodic snapshots

streaming ingestion for near-real-time use cases


A mature platform supports more than one.


---

3. Data zones matter

A common lake/lakehouse pattern uses zones such as:

raw / bronze

clean / silver

curated / gold


This gives the enterprise a way to preserve source fidelity while still producing trusted curated datasets.


---

4. Definitions need ownership

If “active user,” “net revenue,” or “fraud event” means different things in different dashboards, the platform has failed.

That is why:

semantic definitions

model contracts

dbt-style transformation ownership

data cataloging


are as important as storage technology.


---

5. Data quality must be enforced, not assumed

A mature platform validates:

schema conformance

null thresholds

uniqueness

referential integrity expectations

freshness

volume anomalies


That is what makes analytics trustworthy.


---

6. Security and access patterns differ from app workloads

Data access often needs:

role-based access by domain

row/column-level restrictions

PII masking

audit logging

dataset classification

restricted movement between raw and curated layers


This must align with the compliance-aware posture established earlier.


---

7. The data platform should support both analytics and ML

Layer 11 introduced MLOps. This layer provides the broader data backbone that ML systems depend on:

feature inputs

training datasets

event history

governed offline stores


That connection should be explicit.


---

D. Folder structure

A realistic enterprise data platform structure:

data-platform/
├── ingestion/
│   ├── cdc/
│   │   ├── debezium/
│   │   ├── dms/
│   │   └── connectors/
│   ├── events/
│   │   ├── frontend/
│   │   ├── backend/

│   └── batch/
│       ├── exports/
│       └── schedules/
├── lake/
│   ├── raw/
│   ├── clean/
│   ├── curated/
│   └── retention-policy.md
├── warehouse/
│   ├── dbt/
│   │   ├── models/
│   │   │   ├── staging/
│   │   │   ├── marts/
│   │   │   └── finance/
│   │   ├── tests/
│   │   ├── macros/
│   │   └── sources.yml
│   └── semantic/
├── orchestration/
│   ├── airflow/
│   ├── argo-workflows/

├── governance/

│   ├── lineage/

│   └── pipeline-slos/

    ├── data-contracts.md
    ├── naming-standards.md
    └── ownership.md

For GitOps integration:

gitops-repo/
├── data/
│   ├── airflow/
│   ├── spark-jobs/
│   ├── quality/
│   └── monitoring/


---

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files

1. Reference architecture choices

A pragmatic AWS-centric but portable-by-pattern data architecture:

Source OLTP: Amazon RDS PostgreSQL

CDC: AWS DMS or Debezium/Kafka Connect

Object storage lake: S3

Warehouse / Lake query: Snowflake, Redshift, BigQuery equivalent, or Trino/Athena depending on strategy

Transforms: dbt

Orchestration: Airflow or Argo Workflows

Catalog / lineage: DataHub, OpenMetadata, or managed equivalent

Quality: Great Expectations / dbt tests / Soda / custom checks


I will show AWS-first examples while keeping the pattern portable.


---

2. CDC replication task concept

AWS DMS-style task definition concept

cdc:
  source:
    type: postgres
    endpoint: prod-rds-postgres.internal
    database: appdb
  target:
    type: s3
    bucket: prod-data-lake-raw
    prefix: cdc/postgres/
  tables:
    include:
      - public.users
      - public.orders
      - public.payments
  mode:
    full_load: true
    cdc: true

In a real AWS implementation this would be a DMS replication instance/task, but the logical contract is what matters.


---

3. Example event schema for frontend/backend analytics

ingestion/events/schemas/user_signup_event.json

{
  "$schema": "http://json-schema.org/draft-07/schema#",
  "title": "user_signup_event",
  "type": "object",
  "required": [
    "event_name",
    "event_time",
    "user_id",
    "source",
    "environment"
  ],
  "properties": {
    "event_name": {
      "type": "string",
      "enum": ["user_signup"]
    },
    "event_time": {
      "type": "string",
      "format": "date-time"
    },
    "user_id": {
      "type": "string"
    },
    "source": {
      "type": "string",
      "enum": ["frontend", "backend"]
    },
    "environment": {
      "type": "string"
    },
    "campaign_id": {
      "type": ["string", "null"]
    },
    "country_code": {
      "type": ["string", "null"]
    }
  }
}

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: Data Platform & Analytics

21493: cdc: source: type: postgres
- Ingestion: Implements Change Data Capture (CDC) to move data from OLTP to the lakehouse without impacting production database performance or locking tables.

21588: s3://prod-data-lake/raw/
- Zoning: Separates source fidelity (Raw) from business logic (Curated), allowing analysts to rebuild the state of the system from historical source snapshots.

21619: warehouse/dbt/models/sources.yml
- Governance: Treats analytics transformations as code, applying version control and automated quality tests (dbt test) to critical financial and product datasets.

---

This supports event contract validation and downstream trust.


---

4. Django analytics event publish example

import json
from datetime import datetime, timezone

def emit_signup_event(user_id: str, campaign_id: str | None, country_code: str | None) -> dict:
    event = {
        "event_name": "user_signup",
        "event_time": datetime.now(timezone.utc).isoformat(),
        "user_id": user_id,
        "source": "backend",
        "environment": "prod",
        "campaign_id": campaign_id,
        "country_code": country_code,
    }
    # send to event bus / stream / queue
    return event

In production, this would publish to a durable stream or queue, not just return a dict.


---

5. S3 lake zone structure

s3://prod-data-lake/
├── raw/
│   ├── cdc/
│   │   └── postgres/
│   ├── events/
│   │   ├── frontend/
│   │   └── backend/
│   └── batch/
├── clean/
│   ├── users/
│   ├── orders/
│   └── events/
└── curated/
    ├── finance/
    ├── product/
    ├── growth/
    └── ml/

Why this matters:

raw preserves source truth

clean standardizes and de-duplicates

curated serves trusted domain datasets



---

6. dbt source and model configuration

warehouse/dbt/models/sources.yml

version: 2

sources:
  - name: raw_app
    database: analytics
    schema: raw
    tables:
      - name: users
      - name: orders
      - name: payments
      - name: user_signup_events

warehouse/dbt/models/staging/stg_users.sql

select
    cast(id as varchar) as user_id,
    email,
    created_at,
    status,
    country_code
from {{ source('raw_app', 'users') }}

warehouse/dbt/models/marts/fct_daily_signups.sql

select
    date_trunc('day', created_at) as signup_day,
    country_code,
    count(*) as signup_count
from {{ ref('stg_users') }}
group by 1, 2

warehouse/dbt/models/marts/schema.yml

version: 2

models:
  - name: fct_daily_signups
    columns:
      - name: signup_day
        tests:
          - not_null
      - name: signup_count
        tests:
          - not_null

This is the right enterprise pattern: source → staging → marts with tests.


---

7. Data quality checks

governance/data-quality/orders_quality.yaml

dataset: curated.finance.orders

checks:
  - name: not_null_order_id
    type: not_null
    column: order_id

  - name: unique_order_id
    type: unique
    column: order_id

  - name: positive_amount
    type: expression
    expression: "amount >= 0"

  - name: freshness_under_60m
    type: freshness
    max_lag_minutes: 60

This can be implemented by Great Expectations, Soda, dbt tests, or an equivalent framework.


---

8. Airflow DAG for daily transformations

orchestration/airflow/dags/daily_analytics_transform.py

from airflow import DAG
from airflow.operators.bash import BashOperator
from datetime import datetime

with DAG(
    dag_id="daily_analytics_transform",
    start_date=datetime(2026, 4, 1),
    schedule="0 * * * *",
    catchup=False,
    tags=["analytics", "dbt"],
) as dag:

    dbt_run = BashOperator(
        task_id="dbt_run",
        bash_command="cd /opt/airflow/dbt && dbt run --target prod",
    )

    dbt_test = BashOperator(
        task_id="dbt_test",
        bash_command="cd /opt/airflow/dbt && dbt test --target prod",
    )

    dbt_run >> dbt_test

This is simple but realistic: transform, then validate.


---

9. Metadata / catalog ownership file

docs/ownership.md

domains:
  product:
    owner: product-analytics
    steward: lead-product-analyst

  finance:
    owner: finance-data
    steward: finance-analytics-manager

  growth:
    owner: growth-engineering
    steward: growth-analytics-lead

datasets:
  curated.finance.orders:
    classification: confidential
    pii: false
    owner_domain: finance

  curated.product.user_activity:
    classification: internal
    pii: true
    owner_domain: product

This supports access control and accountability.


---

10. Access policy concept

governance/access-policies/finance_orders.yaml

dataset: curated.finance.orders

access:
  read:
    - finance-data
    - finance-analytics
    - executive-reporting
  write:
    - finance-etl
  restricted_columns:
    - customer_email
    - billing_address

This shows a governance model that can later map to warehouse-native permissions or catalog policy enforcement.


---

11. Freshness monitoring rule

monitoring/freshness/orders_freshness.yaml

dataset: curated.finance.orders
expected_update_frequency_minutes: 60
alert:
  warning_after_minutes: 90
  critical_after_minutes: 180
owner: finance-data

This is one of the most valuable analytics monitoring controls because stale data often breaks trust before schema breaks do.


---

12. Data pipeline SLA / SLO file

monitoring/pipeline-slos.yaml

pipelines:
  daily_analytics_transform:
    availability_slo: "99.5%"
    freshness_slo: "95% of runs complete within 30 minutes of schedule"
    owner: analytics-platform

  cdc_postgres_to_raw:
    availability_slo: "99.9%"
    lag_slo: "p95 CDC lag under 5 minutes"
    owner: data-platform


---

F. Line-by-line explanation of critical sections

CDC into raw zone

CDC is a key design choice because it lets analytics consume near-real-time operational changes without querying production RDS directly.

Benefits:

offloads analytics pressure from OLTP

preserves change history better

supports near-real-time reporting and ML inputs

creates a clean raw ingest layer



---

Event schemas

Explicit event schemas are important because frontend/backend analytics often degrade when event producers change fields ad hoc.

A schema contract helps with:

versioning

validation

compatibility

catalog documentation

downstream trust



---

Lake zones

The raw / clean / curated zone model is valuable because it separates:

what arrived from source

what was cleaned and standardized

what the business should actually use


This prevents analysts from building important dashboards directly off unstable raw data.


---

dbt staging and marts

This is a classic enterprise transformation pattern.

Staging

Standardize source fields and types.

Marts

Produce business-friendly curated models.

This makes transformation logic:

testable

reviewable

versioned

easier to own by domain



---

Data quality checks

These convert “we hope the data is good” into explicit controls.

Examples:

uniqueness

null checks

positive values

freshness


This is analogous to tests in software engineering.


---

Freshness monitoring

Freshness is often the first thing business stakeholders notice.

A dataset that is technically correct but six hours stale can still create a severe operational issue.

That is why freshness deserves explicit SLOs and alerts.


---

Ownership and classification

A strong data platform must know:

who owns the dataset

who stewards it

whether it contains PII

how it should be accessed


This is essential for compliance and practical governance.


---

G. Commands to run

1. Run dbt transformations

cd warehouse/dbt
dbt run --target prod

What it does

Builds or refreshes the transformation models in the warehouse.


---

2. Run dbt tests

cd warehouse/dbt
dbt test --target prod

What it does

Executes data quality assertions defined in schema and model test files.


---

3. Generate dbt docs

cd warehouse/dbt
dbt docs generate

What it does

Produces model documentation and lineage artifacts for analytics teams.


---

4. Trigger Airflow DAG manually

airflow dags trigger daily_analytics_transform

What it does

Starts the transformation workflow outside the normal schedule.


---

5. Validate JSON event schema

Using a JSON schema validation tool or pipeline step:

python validate_event.py --schema ingestion/events/schemas/user_signup_event.json --payload sample_event.json

What it does

Ensures emitted event payloads conform to the agreed schema contract.


---

6. Check dataset freshness

Warehouse query example:

select max(updated_at) from curated.finance.orders;

What it does

Provides a quick freshness sanity check for a critical dataset.


---

7. Inspect CDC lag

The exact command depends on DMS, Debezium, or Kafka tooling, but operationally this should expose:

current lag

failed tasks

backlog trend



---

H. Production best practices

1. Never run serious analytics directly on production RDS

Use CDC, replicas for transitional cases, or a proper warehouse/lake path.


---

2. Treat data models as code

Use version control, review, tests, CI, and ownership metadata.


---

3. Separate raw from curated

Do not expose unstable ingest tables as primary analytics sources.


---

4. Define ownership for every critical dataset

A dataset without an owner becomes an incident later.


---

5. Enforce data quality checks in pipelines

Quality should be a gate, not a manual guess.


---

6. Monitor freshness, volume, and schema drift

All three matter in analytics operations.


---

7. Keep PII and restricted data classified

Apply masking, column restrictions, and audited access.


---

8. Align curated datasets with business domains

Finance, product, growth, ops, ML.

This scales better than a central team owning every metric definition forever.


---

9. Reuse the platform’s existing controls

Use the same:

CI patterns

GitOps

observability

IAM/IRSA

secrets handling

policy mindset


for data workloads where applicable.


---

I. Common enterprise mistakes

1. BI queries against production OLTP

This is one of the classic anti-patterns.


---

2. No semantic consistency

If every dashboard defines “active user” differently, trust collapses.


---

3. Raw tables exposed as trusted sources

This causes repeated business confusion.


---

4. No freshness alerts

Stakeholders often notice stale data before engineers do.


---

5. No dataset ownership

Issues then bounce between analytics, engineering, and platform teams.


---

6. Manual SQL pipelines with no version control

This creates fragile operations and no audit trail.


---

7. Treating data quality as optional

Bad data creates business incidents just as real as app outages.


---

8. No classification or masking for sensitive fields

This creates compliance and privacy exposure.


---

J. Interview explanation (Senior/Principal level)

A strong Principal-level explanation would sound like this:

> I separate operational application workloads from analytical workloads by moving transactional data into a governed analytics platform using CDC, event ingestion, and batch pipelines rather than running analytics directly on the production database. I organize the lake or warehouse into raw, clean, and curated zones, use dbt-style transformations and tests to produce trusted business datasets, and define ownership, classification, and freshness expectations for each critical dataset. The platform also supports both BI and ML by exposing curated marts and governed offline data products, while monitoring lag, freshness, schema quality, and pipeline SLOs so analytics becomes an engineered capability instead of an informal side effect of the app.

Layer 13 outcome

After this layer is added, the architecture now also includes:

a governed analytics data platform

CDC and event-driven ingestion patterns

lake/lakehouse zoning

dbt-style transformation and testing

data quality and freshness monitoring

ownership and classification for datasets

access policy concepts for sensitive data

a clean bridge between application data and ML/analytics use cases


If you want to keep extending this architecture, the strongest next addition would be Layer 14: API Management & Developer Experience Layer covering API gateways, rate limits, schema governance, internal developer portals, service templates, and self-service platform workflows.

14. API Management & Developer Experience Layer

A. What this layer/tool is

This layer defines how internal and external developers discover, consume, govern, secure, and operate APIs and platform capabilities.

It covers two closely related domains:

API Management

north-south API exposure

authentication and authorization

rate limiting and quotas

request validation

versioning and deprecation

API analytics

developer onboarding

internal vs external API segmentation


Developer Experience (DX)

internal developer portal

service templates

self-service workflows

environment provisioning patterns

golden paths

documentation standards

scaffolding and scorecards

platform adoption ergonomics


This layer is what turns the platform from “something experts can operate” into “something many teams can safely use.”


---

B. Why it is needed at this stage

The architecture already includes:

application delivery

Kubernetes platform

GitOps

observability

security controls

DR

MLOps

data platform

team operating model


But large-scale enterprises also need to answer:

how do clients access APIs safely?

how are public and internal APIs governed differently?

how are rate limits enforced?

how do teams publish API contracts?

how do new services get started quickly without rebuilding the same platform wiring?

how do we make the paved road easy enough that teams want to use it?

how do we standardize ownership, docs, alerts, runbooks, SLOs, and security posture at service creation time?


Without this layer, teams often end up with:

inconsistent API authentication patterns

undocumented internal APIs

weak versioning discipline

shadow ingress paths

uneven rate limiting

poor consumer onboarding

copy-paste service bootstrapping

slow developer onboarding

weak platform adoption


This layer is needed because Principal-level platform design must optimize for both control and developer throughput.


---

C. Real-world enterprise reasoning

1. API exposure is not just ingress

An ALB ingress gets traffic into the cluster, but API management requires much more:

authn/authz policy

consumer identity

quota enforcement

request/response policy

schema standards

analytics by client/team/product

deprecation communication


So API management needs a control plane above raw ingress.


---

2. Internal and external APIs should not be governed identically

A mature enterprise distinguishes:

public/customer APIs

partner APIs

internal product APIs

platform APIs

admin-only APIs


Each needs different controls for:

security

rate limits

documentation

approval

change management

observability



---

3. Developer experience is a reliability multiplier

Poor DX creates:

workarounds

shadow tooling

weak standards adoption

accidental security gaps

platform resentment


Strong DX creates:

fast onboarding

stronger standardization

fewer support tickets

safer defaults

better consistency across teams


This is why DX is not a “nice to have.” It is a force multiplier.


---

4. Golden paths must be executable, not just documented

The best platform teams do not only publish docs saying:

add requests/limits

add probes

add ServiceMonitor

add ownership metadata


They provide templates, generators, pipelines, and scorecards that make those controls appear by default.

That is the real meaning of platform engineering maturity.


---

5. API schemas are part of governance

API contracts should be versioned and reviewable through:

OpenAPI for REST

AsyncAPI for event-driven interfaces

protobuf/IDL where relevant

backward compatibility checks

deprecation policies


This gives application and platform teams a shared contract.


---

6. The internal developer portal becomes the front door to the platform

Instead of every engineer asking:

how do I create a service?

where are the dashboards?

who owns this API?

what does this service depend on?

how do I request a database?


They should be able to use a self-service portal.

A common enterprise implementation:

Backstage as internal developer portal



---

D. Folder structure

A realistic enterprise structure:

developer-platform/
├── api-management/
│   ├── gateway/
│   │   ├── kong/
│   │   ├── apisix/
│   │   ├── ambassador/
│   │   └── ingress-policies/
│   ├── contracts/
│   │   ├── openapi/
│   │   ├── asyncapi/
│   │   └── linting/
│   ├── policies/

E. Copy-paste ready configuration files

1. OpenAPI contract example

app-repo/api/openapi.yaml

openapi: 3.0.3
info:
  title: Fullstack App API
  version: 2.8.4
  description: Public API for the fullstack application

servers:
  - url: https://api.example.com

paths:
  /api/v1/users/{id}:
    get:
      summary: Get user by ID
      operationId: getUser
      parameters:
        - name: id
          in: path
          required: true
          schema:
            type: string
      responses:
        '200':
          description: Successful response
          content:
            application/json:
              schema:
                $ref: '#/components/schemas/User'
        '404':
          description: User not found

components:
  schemas:
    User:
      type: object
      required:
        - id
        - email
      properties:
        id:
          type: string
        email:
          type: string
          format: email
        country_code:
          type: string
          nullable: true

---

Line-by-line Technical Commentary: API Management & DX

22607: openapi.yaml
- Contract: Serves as the single source of truth for the API interface, enabling automated client generation, documentation (Swagger), and gateway-level validation.

22684: kind: Application (kong)
- Exposure: Leverages a high-performance Kubernetes-native gateway for north-south traffic, centralizing authn/authz and rate-limiting at the entry point.

22803: kind: Component (backstage)
- Discovery: Provides a unified developer portal ("Backstage") to reduce cognitive load and fragmentation, giving engineers one place to find every service, API, and dashboard.

---

This should live with the service and be version-controlled.


---

2. API lint CI step

ci/scripts/lint-openapi.sh

#!/usr/bin/env bash
set -euo pipefail

spectral lint app-repo/api/openapi.yaml

This ensures basic API contract hygiene in CI.


---

3. Kong-based API gateway ArgoCD Application

A realistic choice for Kubernetes-native API management is Kong Gateway, though Apigee, Azure API Management, AWS API Gateway, or internal meshes may also be valid depending on external API needs.

gitops-repo/platform/api-gateway/kong.yaml

apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: kong
  namespace: argocd
spec:
  project: platform
  source:
    repoURL: https://charts.konghq.com
    chart: kong
    targetRevision: 2.42.0
    helm:
      values: |
        env:
          database: "off"
        ingressController:
          enabled: true
        proxy:
          type: LoadBalancer
          annotations:
            service.beta.kubernetes.io/aws-load-balancer-scheme: internet-facing
        resources:
          requests:
            cpu: 250m
            memory: 512Mi
          limits:
            cpu: 2
            memory: 2Gi
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: api-gateway
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true
    syncOptions:
      - CreateNamespace=true

For AWS-first external APIs, an alternative pattern is:

CloudFront/WAF

API Gateway

VPC Link / ALB / NLB

EKS backend


But for Kubernetes-centric portability and self-managed control, Kong is a credible enterprise option.


---

4. Rate limit policy example

Kong plugin example

apiVersion: configuration.konghq.com/v1
kind: KongPlugin
metadata:
  name: fullstack-api-rate-limit
  namespace: app-prod
plugin: rate-limiting
config:
  minute: 300
  hour: 10000
  policy: local

And attach it to an ingress/service:

apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: fullstack-api
  namespace: app-prod
  annotations:
    konghq.com/plugins: fullstack-api-rate-limit
spec:
  ingressClassName: kong
  rules:
    - host: api.example.com
      http:
        paths:
          - path: /api
            pathType: Prefix
            backend:
              service:
                name: backend
                port:
                  number: 8000


---

5. API authentication policy example

Illustrative approach using OIDC/JWT validation at the gateway layer.

apiVersion: configuration.konghq.com/v1
kind: KongPlugin
metadata:
  name: oidc-auth
  namespace: app-prod
plugin: openid-connect
config:
  issuer: "https://auth.example.com/"
  client_id:
    - "api-gateway-client"
  scopes_required:
    - "api.read"

This centralizes common auth patterns rather than forcing every service to reinvent them.


---

6. Backstage catalog entity for the service

app-repo/catalog-info.yaml

apiVersion: backstage.io/v1alpha1
kind: Component
metadata:
  name: fullstack-app
  description: Django and React full-stack application
  tags:
    - django
    - react
    - kubernetes
    - production
  annotations:
    github.com/project-slug: examplecorp/fullstack-app
    argocd/app-name: fullstack-app
    grafana/dashboard-selector: fullstack-app
    jenkins.io/job-full-name: fullstack-app/main
spec:
  type: service
  lifecycle: production
  owner: group:customer-platform
  system: customer-experience
  providesApis:
    - fullstack-app-api
  dependsOn:
    - resource:prod-postgres
    - resource:prod-eks


---

7. Backstage API entity

app-repo/catalog-api.yaml

apiVersion: backstage.io/v1alpha1
kind: API
metadata:
  name: fullstack-app-api
  description: Public API for the fullstack app
spec:
  type: openapi
  lifecycle: production
  owner: group:customer-platform
  definition:
    $text: ./api/openapi.yaml

8. Backstage system and resource entities

apiVersion: backstage.io/v1alpha1
kind: System
metadata:
  name: customer-experience
spec:
  owner: group:customer-platform

apiVersion: backstage.io/v1alpha1
kind: Resource
metadata:
  name: prod-postgres
spec:
  type: database
  owner: group:platform-engineering
  system: customer-experience


---

9. Backstage scaffolder template for a web service

backstage/templates/web-service/template.yaml

apiVersion: scaffolder.backstage.io/v1beta3
kind: Template
metadata:
  name: golden-path-web-service
  title: Golden Path Web Service
  description: Create a production-ready web service using the enterprise platform standard
spec:
  owner: group:platform-engineering
  type: service

  parameters:
    - title: Service metadata
      required:
        - name
        - owner
      properties:
        name:
          type: string
          title: Service name
        owner:
          type: string
          title: Owning team

  steps:
    - id: fetch-base
      name: Fetch base template
      action: fetch:template
      input:
        url: ./skeleton
        values:
          name: ${{ parameters.name }}
          owner: ${{ parameters.owner }}

    - id: publish
      name: Publish repository
      action: publish:github
      input:
        repoUrl: github.com?owner=examplecorp&repo=${{ parameters.name }}

    - id: register
      name: Register in catalog
      action: catalog:register
      input:
        repoContentsUrl: ${{ steps.publish.output.repoContentsUrl }}
        catalogInfoPath: /catalog-info.yaml

The template skeleton should already include:

Helm chart

CI pipeline

catalog-info.yaml

owner.yaml

slo.yaml

support.yaml

default probes

resource limits

NetworkPolicies

ServiceMonitor

ExternalSecret reference


That is the executable golden path.

10. Scorecard definition example

scorecards/service-maturity.yaml

serviceMaturity:
  required:
    - ownerMetadataPresent
    - sloDefined
    - supportMetadataPresent
    - openApiPublished
    - alertsConfigured
    - dashboardConfigured
    - pdbPresent
    - networkPolicyPresent
    - resourceLimitsPresent
    - externalSecretOrApprovedAlt
  optional:
    - canaryEnabled
    - syntheticChecksEnabled
    - costDashboardLinked

This can be rendered in the developer portal as a readiness score.


---

11. API versioning policy example

docs/api-versioning-policy.md

# API versioning policy

## Rules
- breaking changes require a new major API version
- additive non-breaking changes may remain within current version
- deprecated endpoints must be announced before removal
- public APIs require minimum 90-day deprecation window
- internal APIs require documented migration path before removal

## Version style
- REST paths: /api/v1, /api/v2
- schema versions documented in OpenAPI


---

12. Deprecation policy example

docs/deprecation-policy.md

# API deprecation policy

## Required for deprecation
- affected consumers identified
- migration guidance published
- sunset date defined
- alerts/logging added to deprecated endpoint usage
- owner responsible for communication

## Sunset windows
- public customer API: 90 days minimum
- partner API: 120 days minimum
- internal API: 30 days minimum unless critical dependency exists


---

13. Self-service workflow example

docs/self-service-workflows.md

# Self-service workflows

## Create a new service
1. Use Backstage golden-path template
2. Select workload type
3. Fill owner/team/system metadata
4. Generated repo includes CI, Helm, GitOps, security, observability baselines
5. Submit PR for namespace and environment onboarding
6. Pass PRR before production

## Request a managed database
1. Open platform workflow form
2. Select data class and environment
3. Security review if confidential or regulated
4. Terraform module generated through approved path


---

F. Line-by-line explanation of critical sections

OpenAPI in repo

Keeping the API spec in the same repo as the service is important because it makes:

contract changes reviewable

CI validation possible

portal publishing easy

versioning tied to service changes


This is much better than a separate wiki page that drifts.


---

API gateway layer

A gateway like Kong gives:

centralized auth integration

rate limiting

request policy

consumer analytics

API-specific routing control


This should be used selectively. Not every internal path needs full gateway features, but external and partner APIs usually benefit.


---

Rate limiting

Rate limiting is both a security control and a reliability control.

It protects:

upstream app capacity

abusive or buggy clients

dependency cost explosion

fairness among API consumers


In enterprise environments, rate limits are often tier-aware by consumer class.


---

OIDC/JWT validation at the gateway

This reduces repeated auth boilerplate across services and creates a more consistent security posture.

Important nuance: application-level authorization still matters. Gateway auth should complement, not fully replace, domain authorization checks.


---

Backstage catalog entities

These make service metadata discoverable in one place:

repo

owner

dependencies

API contracts

dashboards

CI jobs

Argo app linkage


This reduces operational hunting during incidents and onboarding.


---

Backstage scaffolder

This is the single strongest DX mechanism in this layer.

Why:

creates standardized services quickly

bakes in controls from day one

reduces copy-paste

improves platform adoption

shortens time-to-first-production-safe-service


This is what mature platform engineering looks like in practice.


---

Scorecards

Scorecards turn platform maturity into visible, actionable signals.

Instead of asking: “Is this service platform-compliant?”

You can answer with concrete checks:

ownership present

SLO present

alerts wired

API published

rollout safety present


That is much more scalable.


---

Versioning and deprecation policies

These are often missing in otherwise strong platforms.

A mature API program needs:

compatibility rules

consumer communication

sunset windows

usage tracking of deprecated paths


That is how you avoid breaking downstream teams or customers by surprise.


---

G. Commands to run

1. Lint the OpenAPI spec

spectral lint app-repo/api/openapi.yaml

What it does

Validates the API contract for style and correctness before merge.


---

2. Render the API docs locally

Depending on tooling, for example Swagger UI:

docker run --rm -p 8081:8080 -e SWAGGER_JSON=/api/openapi.yaml -v $(pwd)/app-repo/api:/api swaggerapi/swagger-ui

What it does

Lets developers preview API documentation locally.


---

3. Validate Backstage catalog entity

cat app-repo/catalog-info.yaml

In practice, Backstage or CI validation plugins can check schema correctness.

What it does

Confirms service metadata exists and is ready for portal registration.


---

4. Register a component in Backstage

This is usually done by scaffolder or ingestion, but conceptually:

curl -X POST https://backstage.internal.example.com/api/catalog/entities \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d @app-repo/catalog-info.yaml

What it does

Adds or updates a component in the internal developer catalog.


---

5. Apply Kong plugin config

kubectl apply -f rate-limit-plugin.yaml
kubectl apply -f oidc-plugin.yaml

What it does

Adds API gateway policies for rate limiting and authentication.


---

6. Check gateway resources

kubectl -n api-gateway get pods,svc,ingress

What it does

Verifies the gateway control/data plane is healthy.


---

7. Create a new service from template

In practice this is done through Backstage UI, but the workflow results in:

new repo

standard CI

Helm chart

GitOps-ready manifests

service metadata



---

H. Production best practices

1. Treat APIs as products

They need:

owners

contracts

versioning

deprecation policy

analytics

support expectations



---

2. Keep contracts in source control

OpenAPI, AsyncAPI, protobufs, and event schemas should be versioned.


---

3. Use a gateway for external and partner APIs

This provides consistent enforcement for auth, quotas, and policy.


---

4. Make the golden path executable

Templates and scaffolding should generate compliant services by default.


---

5. Invest in a developer portal

Discovery, ownership, and self-service are major multipliers at enterprise scale.


---

6. Surface maturity and compliance visibly

Scorecards drive adoption and improvement better than hidden checklists.


---

7. Track deprecated API usage

Do not remove old endpoints blindly.


---

8. Keep portal metadata synchronized with reality

If the portal becomes stale, trust disappears quickly.


---

I. Common enterprise mistakes

1. Using ingress as the only API control plane

This misses many API product needs.


---

2. No API contract linting

Breaking or inconsistent changes then slip into releases.


---

3. Developer portal as a documentation graveyard

A portal must be operationally integrated, not just a static index.


---

4. Golden path exists only in slides/docs

If teams cannot instantiate it, they will fork their own approach.


---

5. No deprecation process

This creates painful downstream breakage.


---

6. No ownership metadata for APIs

Incidents and migration efforts become slower.


---

7. Excessive central approval for basic workflows

Self-service should be the norm for standard platform actions.


---

J. Interview explanation (Senior/Principal level)

A strong Principal-level explanation would sound like this:

> I extend the platform with both an API management layer and an internal developer experience layer. On the API side, I treat APIs as governed products with versioned OpenAPI contracts, centralized authentication and rate limiting for exposed interfaces, and explicit deprecation policy. On the DX side, I use an internal developer portal such as Backstage to make services, APIs, dependencies, dashboards, and ownership discoverable, and I provide executable golden-path templates so new services are created with CI, Helm, GitOps, observability, and security baselines already wired in. That approach increases consistency, reduces support burden, and makes the safest platform path the easiest one for developers to use.

Layer 14 outcome

After this layer is added, the architecture now also includes:

API contract governance

external/internal API management patterns

gateway-based auth and rate limiting

API versioning and deprecation policy

internal developer portal model

executable golden-path templates

service/API catalog entities

maturity scorecards and self-service workflows


This brings the architecture fully into enterprise platform engineering + developer platform territory.
